I 
PREFACE 
DANSIH TITLE 
Et studie af hvorledes slutbrugerens og samfundets interesser begge kan includes i 
ingeniørsudviklingsprocesser. 
 
ENGLISH TITLE 
A study on how to include both end-user and societal interests in engineering 
development. 
 
DESCRIPTION 
This project sets out to explore how to include a societal perspective in the 
engineering development process. This is going to unite the interests of the end-user 
and society in the interaction between the end-user and the product. The theoretical 
foundation will be laid by a scientific literature review and the empirical data will 
come from interviews of engineers and employees from the Technical and 
Environmental department in municipalities 
 
 
 
 
 
Project: 
 
Master’s thesis in M.Sc. Design and Innovation  
Author: 
 
Emilia Herrik  
Supervisor:  
Philip Cash 
Submission:  
29.04.2016 
Department:  
Management Engineering 
University: 
 
Technical University of Denmark, DTU 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Emilia Herrik, s102964  
  
April 29th 2016 
==PAGE:3==
 
II
ABSTRACT 
The purpose of the project was to explore if societal interests can be included in an 
engineering development process and thereby influence on the interaction between 
the end-user and the product in a society beneficial way. Furthermore the project 
looked into the interaction between three main actors: the engineering company, the 
end-user and society. The focus has been on products exclusively. 
 
A scientific literature review of the current models that are developed to support 
engineers in the product development process showed a gap in the interaction 
between the three actors. No work model/method were including all three actors 
interests. 
Interviews were conducted to examine if the gap also existed in the real world 
development process. The interviews were conducted with engineers who were 
working with product development to clarify the real world developing process and 
the interests considered in that. Interviews were conducted with employees from the 
department of Technique and Environment from different municipalities as well, to 
clarify what a development process looked like in a public institution that is forced to 
consider societal goals to some extend.  
As well as the literature review, the interviews showed a gap in the interaction 
between the three actors and their interests. It also showed that the concerns of the 
end-user and society often are conflicting. 
  
On the foundation of the literature review and the interviews a set of guidelines was 
developed. The guidelines give the engineers a tool to use in the product development 
process and in that way include societal interests. A guideline consists beside the 
actual guideline of an example to clarify the meaning and a suggestion on how to 
apply the guideline to a product.  
 
The guidelines create the first step towards a model/method that engineers can use in 
their product development work to meet social interests. Further research should be 
done in order to test the guidelines and thereby make them fit the engineering 
development work even better.

4 
1. INTRODUCTION 
 
1.1 GOOD DESIGN… 
If a design is good or bad can be hard to give a definite answer to. The aesthetic part is 
impossible to define as good or bad design, because it is a biased matter. The functional part 
is a bit easier to establish as good or bad design. A door for instance will have to be 
categorised as a good design all-functionally. All doors consist of a plate to cover a hole, 
hinges and a handle. One of the most obvious arguments for explaining why a door is a good 
design, is that doors has been produces in countless years and still is produced in great 
numbers all over the world. Still consisting of a plate to cover a hole, hinges and a handle. 
This implies that the door is a profitable and good design for a company, because it still can 
be sold. Another good argument is that almost every building in the entire world has some 
kind of door. It can thereby be categorised as a good design for the end-users as well. The 
user buys the doors and uses them every day. 
 
1.2 …OR BAD DESIGN. 
It is though not always that the door sends the right signal on how to open it. Have you ever 
dragged in a handle that was supposed to be pushed? Or been in doubt if the door actually 
was locked? Is a door that needs a sign saying ‘PUSH’ a good design? A fire door is only a 
good functioning design if panicking people manage to open it in a critical situation. A good 
fire door design is of cause important for the end-user – they want to get out alive. It is also 
important for the company – they will get sued if anything bad happens because of their 
door. A well functioning door is important for yet another actor - society. Society wants to 
have healthy citizens who can work, pay taxes and get everything to work in an everyday 
life. Society tries to make that happen by making laws and recommending CSR-strategies for 
companies. This will for instance ensure that the door is produced in a non-toxic material and 
that the door isn’t too heavy to operate and thereby expose the end-user for danger. But can it 
ensure correct and optimal use?  
Many different informants discuss the aspect of good and bad functional design according to 
interaction between the end-user of the product and the product. This aspect has been 
researched by behavioural scientists and behavioural economists and has laid the foundation 
for design-trends like nudging and choice architecture. Nudging has among other things been 
==PAGE:11==
 
5 
used as a way to solve many problems in public spaces, by changing the end-users behaviour 
in the user phase.  
 
1.3 SOCIETY AND END-USERS CONFLICTING 
When asking ourselves what is good and what is bad design, many possible answers can be 
given depending on which perspective to answer from. Three of the main players that 
interact with a product are, the engineering company, the end-user and society. They have 
some similar interests, but also some contending interests.  
In 2013 the Danish government developed a resource strategy called ‘Denmark without 
waste’ to minimize the ‘use and burn’ mentality. The government claims that this strategy 
both will save money and natural resources (Miljøstyrelsen 2013). Society wants the users to 
sort their waste; unfortunately this isn’t what the users do. An analysis of waste from 14th of 
December 2015 shows that the amount of reusable materials in household garbage collection 
has increased from 2011 to 2015 (Steffensen et al 2015). This is an example of a mismatch 
between the end-user and society.   
 
1.4 AIM OF PROJECT 
The aim of this project will be to examine the correlation between the engineering company, 
the end-user and society according to the user phase. The focus of the project is exclusively 
on products and will be on how engineers consider the end-user and society in a product 
development process. Furthermore the aim will be to investigate how to fulfil the needs for 
all three actors during the product developing process to create good functional design for all 
three actors.  
 
1.5 REASEARCH QUESTIONS 
Based on the description of the project and the project aim, the following research questions 
has been formulated: 
 
How can engineering companies be supported in influencing the interaction between the 
end-user and the product in a way that consider company, end-user and societal interests?  
 
 
==PAGE:12==
 
6 
From this 4 sub questions were formulated: 
 
1. How are the end-user perspective and the societal perspective currently considered 
in the theory about engineering development processes?  
2. How are the end-user perspective and the societal perspective currently considered 
in the engineering development process?  
3. What are the similarities and differences between the end-users and societies 
interests? 
4. How can engineers be supported in including a societal perspective in product 
development? 
 
1.6 REPORT STRUCTURE 
This thesis includes 6 chapters. During these chapters the research questions are going to be 
answered. Chapter 1 is the introduction and includes a description of the underlying 
construct, the aim of the project and the research questions. 
Chapter 2 outlines the theoretical framework for how the three main actors interact through 
models and thereby creates the theoretical foundation for the thesis. The review further 
describes a gap that appears between the actors.  
Chapter 3 introduces the research method chosen for this project and reviews the study 
approach and central considerations related to the consistency of the study. 
Chapter 4 analyses the results of the empirical work according to the project aim. 
Chapter 5 brings together the insights from the literature review and the analysis in a 
discussion that leads to the development of a set of guidelines. The genesis of the guidelines 
is discussed as well and the limitations and implications according to these. 
Chapter 6 presents a conclusion answering the research question and describes further work 
for the project. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:13==
 
7 
2. LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
2.1 INTRODUCTION 
This literature review will give an overview of existing models that are used in engineering 
development processes. This overview will enlighten if any gap appears in the models and 
show how the models make the actors interact. 
The three main actors will be the engineering company, the end-user and society and it will 
be according to these actors the models will be represented. 
The literature review is therefor divided into three main categories; company specific models 
(sec. 2.2), company-user interaction (sec. 2.3) and society perspectives and interactions (sec. 
2.4). A visual showing of each model described in the literature review can be seen in 
appendix 24. 
 
The research starts out by exploring which kind of models that are developed to be used by 
engineers without any involvement of end-user or society (sec. 2.2).  
This leads to see if there are any models that are developed to consider end-users or society - 
either by observing or by including end-users. This will show how the end-user influences 
the engineering development process (sec. 2.3).  
The last main section (sec. 2.4) is focussing on how society currently influences the 
development of new products and if there are models that accumulate all three actors at once. 
Figure 1 shows the starting framework and how they can interact. 
 
 
Figure 1 - The starting framework

USER
COMPANY

==PAGE:14==

2.2 COMPANY SPECIFIC MODELS 
The following section is seen from the engineers’ point of view to enlighten which kind of 
models the theory put at the engineers disposal, without involving external involvement.  
The methods and models in the following paragraph are divided into three categories;  
 
• Work models that explain how a holistic work process could proceed from 
beginning to end.  
• Explanations that explains a specific field, perspective or approach, but doesn’t 
suggest how to include it in a development process.  
• Guidelines that sets focus on a specific approach and functions as a check list for the 
developers.  
 
2.2.1 WORK MODELS 
2.2.1.1$Generic$Product$Development$Process$model$
There has been developed many models for designers and engineers to help in the process of 
developing new products. A lot of these work models are similar to Ulrich and Eppinger’s 
Generic Product Development Process model (GPDP). The aim of this model is to lead the 
engineer through the product development and get a technical good result. The model 
includes 6 steps and describes a typical engineering process from the planning process to 
production ramp-up. The model focuses on the technical aspect of the product development 
and production, and considers the end-user interaction minimal. There are only two of the 
steps that suggest end-user involvement of some kind. The first of these suggests end-user 
involvement by either interview, focus groups, written surveys, and/or observations (Ulrich 
and Eppinger 2008).  
The second step suggesting end-user involvement includes testing of prototypes. The 
prototype testing has two purposes; 1 is to se if it works and this purpose doesn’t include the 
end-user; 2 is to assess how well the customers need are meet (Ulrich and Eppinger 2008).  
Ulrich and Eppinger explain pros and cons about the different end-user involvement 
methods, but are mainly focusing on the expenses and time consumption and not the degree 
of end-user involvement.  
None of the 6 steps refers directly to environmental issues, user safety/health, saving money 
in the user phase or other goals that society might want fulfilled.   
==PAGE:15==
 
9 
2.2.1.2$Quality$Function$Deployment$Method$
Another much used method is the Quality Function Deployment method (QFD). This model 
represents a group of models that combine the customer/end-user requirements with the 
engineering characteristics of a product and thereby see how to meet most of the end-user 
requirements (Cross 2000). The end-user requirements could for instance be that a car had 5 
doors, could drive minimum 25 km/l and so forth (Cross 2000). This shows that end-user 
requirements don’t have anything to do with interaction between the end-user and the 
product. All requirements should on the contrary be measureable in some way (Cross 2000). 
This method can’t reveal any, for the end-user, unknown wishes, because the behaviour isn’t 
taken into account. Even where end-users are involved in this model it is to match business 
to users and not to make the product user-friendlier. If the end-user wants environmentally 
efficient qualities or the engineer is focussing on energy-saving product the societal goals 
will be included, but the model itself doesn’t mention these aspects at all. 
 
2.2.1.3$Storytelling$and$Scenario?Based$design$$$
The first two kinds of models have represented two ways of developing products, both of 
them only considering the end-user interaction to a small degree.  
Vredenburg et al (2002) describes a more user-centered approach within the developing 
process. According to Vredenburg et al (2002) does good design come from a good process.  
GPDP and QFD both are very general and are intended for all kinds of products. Vredenburg 
et al (2002) are working with user-centered design and criticizes the current methodologies 
used for addressing a small range of projects and thereby not suitable for all products. They 
suggest that the way to do a good design, that will fulfil the costumer needs, is to use 
storytelling/personas or scenario-based design  (Vredenburg et al. 2002). Thereby they 
suggest a more specific approach to product development than the two other models. 
Storytelling and scenario-based design are closely related (Moggridge 1993). Storytelling is 
a technique where the designer sets up different personas and through these personas tells 
stories to look at different scenarios. During the work the technique is going to help 
visualizing the solutions (Moggridge 1993).  
Scenario-based design is seen from occurrence instead of the personas point of view.  
Scenarios are also stories and are focussing on the activity instead of the end-user. The 
scenario points at a specific situation and from this the designer comes up with a solution 
(Carrol 1999).  
==PAGE:16==
 
10 
The method describes end-user experiences, can be written at multiple levels, and helps 
design knowledge cumulate across problem instances (Carrol 1999).  
Scenario-based design as well as storytelling is both considering the end-user and the final 
product, but both can be made from the desk and it isn’t mandatory to observe before 
creating a persona or a scenario. So even though Vredenburg et al comes with suggestions on 
how to make a good process it only brings us a bit closer to integrating the end-user in the 
product developing process. The societal perspective is still absence.  
 
The three models above show three levels of considering the end-user, but without actually 
involving the end-user. The focus lays on the technical development of a product and the 
interaction between the end-user and the product is not considered. The next types of models 
will examine if models concerning smaller aspects of product development includes more 
interaction between the three actors. 
 
2.2.2 EXPLANATION MODELS 
The work models were not focussing on the interactions between any of the actors from the 
starting framework (fig. 1). The models in this section are used as smaller tools in the overall 
development process and might give another view on developing. The methods are 
explanations of different mechanisms that could be usable knowledge for the company 
during the product development phase. 
 
2.2.2.1$Levels$of$customer$satisfaction$
Professor Noriaki Kano has developed a model (Kano-model) that helps companies to better 
understanding of customer’s requirements. The Kano model represents a kind of explanation 
model that helps the engineer see how to make the design better. 
 
Kano divides costumer satisfaction, according to product development, into three categories.  
The “Must be’s” factors implies the quality that the customer expects from the product. For 
instance the customer expects a car door to not hit the kerb, when opening it. If it doesn’t the 
customer will remain neutral, if it hits the kerb, the customer will be dissatisfied. If this 
expectation isn’t fulfilled it can lead to customer dissatisfaction (Kano 2015). The QFD 
==PAGE:17==
 
11 
could be a model to solve these problems, but the Kano model only shows that this is the 
lowest level of good design.  
The “one-dimensional” factor implies that the better a product is, the more customer 
satisfaction there will be. For instance the less fuel a car needs the more satisfied the 
customer is (Kano 2015). Still there is no mentioning of any interaction between the actors. 
The third and last factor “attractive” implies that a product stands out from the other 
products and thereby has a possibility to get very high customer satisfaction called the USP 
(unique selling propositions) (Kano 2015). If it isn’t present the customer will not be 
dissatisfied. This is the aspect that will suggest what good design is for the customer (Kano 
2015). This could be the aspect that included interaction between actors to bring the product 
design to a higher level, but because this is an explanation model nothing is mentioned on 
how to obtain this. According to Kano (2015) satisfied customers equal good design. 
Thereby he includes a way of thinking that includes the end-user a lot more than the work 
models. He doesn’t though mention how to include the customer or if it would be an even 
better design if a societal perspective were included as well.   
 
2.2.2.2$Intuitive$$
The next model represents a type of models that brings the interaction between the end-user 
and the product closer together in the developing process. Donald Norman describes good 
design as a design that takes care, planning, thought, and an understanding on how people 
behave (Norman 2013; p. 23). This means that the design should make the user act 
intentionally without any instructions manual. The product would have to be intuitive to the 
end-user.  
Jared M. Spool has made a model that visualises what intuitive means. Spool (2005) starts 
explaining ‘Intuitive’ by making a long beam that represents ‘no knowledge’ in one end and 
‘all knowledge’ in the other end. Between the two extremes there are two arrows that can 
move towards each other. A product can be intuitive in two conditions. The first condition is 
if the ‘current knowledge point’ and the ‘target knowledge point’ is the same and ‘the gap’ 
has disappeared. This means that the actor has all the knowledge needed for using the 
product correctly. For instance a teapot that you immediately know where to grab, where the 
tea will come out and where to put in the tea leaves and the boiled water.  
The other condition is when the ‘current knowledge point’ and the ‘target knowledge point’ 
are a bit apart, but the actor do not notice that the design helps them bridge ‘the gap’ (Spool 
==PAGE:18==
 
12 
2005). This model explains what needs to be achieved to make a good and intuitive design to 
the end-user, so that it for instance wouldn’t be necessary to pull the door handle before 
dragging it. The model is closer to the aim of thesis than any of the previous, because it deals 
with the interaction between end-user and product. It is though still only an explanation to 
the company on what to consider when developing an intuitive product and not a recipe to 
show how. It describes what the intended use would look like, but it doesn’t describe how to 
obtain the interaction between the end-user and the product.  
 
2.2.2.3$Usability$
In comparing to the Kano model and the intuitive model this last explanation model gives 
some keywords of what to consider obtaining end-user satisfaction. Jacob Nielsen (2012) has 
developed keywords on what a product should be capable of to satisfy the end-user. He 
introduces usability and defines it as a quality attribute that determines how easy a user 
interface is to use for an actor (Nielsen 2012). By other means how easy the end-user can 
interact with the product in the intended way. The method is used to improve the design 
process according to ease of use in the user phase (Nielsen 2012).  
There are 5 defining keywords or elements for usability according to Nielsen (2012). 
Learnability describes how easy it is to learn a new technology, so that the actor can 
perform tasks. Efficiency is the effort that has to be done to go from learning about the 
object to performing tasks. The memorability tells how easy it is for the actor to remember 
how the object works. Errors is how many mistakes (mistakes/errors according to the 
designers intentions with the product) the actor makes when using the object. If the object is 
pleasant to use for the actor the satisfaction level is high and the usability likewise (Nielsen 
2012). According to Nielsen (2012) the responsibility for making a product more usable all 
lays with the designer of the product - or in this project the engineer. Even though this way 
of thinking considers the interaction between the end-user and the product, it doesn’t 
mention any societal perspective or how to obtain these five defining elements. If a product 
were fulfilling these elements the end-user would be satisfied, but not necessarily society. 
 
The three explanation models underpins that the end-user is considered very closely even 
though he is not included in the developing work. The explanations focus on important 
issues when developing new products, but none of them tell the engineer how to do it. The 
last kind of models is giving a more comprehensively recipe on how to obtain good design.  
==PAGE:19==
 
13 
2.2.3 DESIGN PRINCIPLES 
Besides the types of models described above, there are a number of design principles to 
secure good design made by different authors with different backgrounds and thereby 
different perspectives. Design principles are lists with recommendations on how to obtain 
good design. 
 
Badly designed devices can induce frustration and anger, a feeling of helplessness and 
despair, and possibly even hate (Norman 2013; p 55). Norman implies here that a poorly 
designed product can cause problems in the interaction between end-user and product and 
thereby that the interaction between these two actors are important.  
To avoid poor design many professors, researchers and designers have made lists of design 
principals that should guide to good design. The lists have been made from different 
perspectives according to the author’s field of work.  
Norman (2013) has made 7 design principals on how to achieve good product design. The 
principles are a combination of how the user should see and interact with the product and 
how to achieve the intended interaction. The principles are written in Normans book ‘The 
Design of Everyday Things’ and the principles are dedicated design of things/products as 
well as this thesis. Because of this focus Normans guidelines are explained in detail 
underneath. 
 
2.2.3.1$Normans’$7$Design$Principles$
The 7 fundamental Norman principles that should be fulfilled to make a good working 
design are: discoverability, feedback, conceptual model, affordances, signifiers, mappings 
and constraints (Norman 2013; p. 72).  
 
The discoverability is when the actor looks at a product and immediately is able to discover 
the possibilities of actions and to know the current state of the product without examine it 
further (Norman 2013). That could for instance be when you look at a bottle: you are able to 
see that it is possible to lift the bottle, unscrew the lid and drink from it. The product as a 
whole should communicate right, so the user will know how it works only by looking at it. 
The individual parts of the product should though also be able to communicate the intended 
message that makes the actor know how the product works just by looking at it (Demirbilek 
==PAGE:20==
 
14 
& Sener 2003). This part lies before the end-user – product interaction, but is important for 
the interaction to be right. 
 
Feedback is information given in the situation to secure that the action is being done 
properly. First kind of feedback is what could be called ‘routine feedback’; it generates 
information on whether the action being done is working or not (Duhigg 2012). The routine 
feedback can be seen as an active action. The second type of feedback is what could be 
called ‘success feedback’; it tells whether the action has been successfully done (Duhigg 
2012). The success feedback can be seen as a passive action. For instance when you brush 
your teeth you do it to get clean teeth. The foam is the ‘routine feedback’; it will tell you that 
you are in the doing of cleaning your teeth. The foam isn’t helping cleaning your teeth, but is 
a good indicator for telling that your teeth are getting clean. When you are done brushing 
your teeth, the smooth surface of the teeth and the fresh taste is the success feedback. It tells 
you that you have done a proper job (Duhigg 2012). This part is important in the interaction 
phase between end-user and product, because the und-user might start using the product in 
an unintended way. 
 
A good conceptual model makes it easier to understand the product and creates a feeling of 
control when using it. This will give the person using the product a feeling of safety in the 
using phase and there will be no doubt about how it works and what will happen when it is 
operated. The conceptual model enhances both the discoverability and the feedback. The 
conceptual model is a kind of storytelling, based on our knowledge and earlier experiences 
and comes from our predisposition to find explanations. We create stories and make up 
relationships to try to give ourselves a view of the situation. A good conceptual model allows 
us to predict the effects of our actions. This part is also important in the interaction phase 
between end-user and product, and focuses on the perception of the product. If the concept 
model is unclear to the end-user this might cause unintended use. 
 
The relationship between the physical product and the end-user is, by Norman (2013) called 
affordance. For instance a chair is supposed to be sat on, therefor it affords sitting (Norman 
2013). Whether an affordance exists depends upon the properties of the object as well as the 
agent. Affordances can exist even if they are not visible. When an affordance is perceived it 
is possible for the end-user to figure out what actions are possible and what actions are not, 
==PAGE:21==
 
15 
without any instructions. Here Norman explicitly sets focus on the interaction between end-
user and product. 
 
Signifiers are the signalling component of affordances. Well-designed signifiers ensure that 
the discoverability and the feedback are communicated in an understandable way on the 
product (Norman 2013). You know immediately what to do when you see a handle – you can 
put your hand around it and push it down. Signifiers can both be signs, arrows and diagrams 
telling what to do, but it can also be the fashioning of the product (Norman 2013).  
Akrich (1992) also describes signifiers and affordances. Akrich (1992) explains the 
relationship between the product, the end-user and the designer using the word script. Scripts 
are the communicating part of the design that the designer puts into the product to get the 
end-user to use the product in a curtain way. Scripts are what Norman (2013) calls signifiers. 
De-scription (Akrich 1992) is the way the user construes the communication, the relationship 
that Norman (2013) calls affordance. Semiotics is closely related to signifiers. Product 
semantics is the study of the symbolic qualities of man-made forms in the context of their 
use and the application of this knowledge to industrial design (Krippendorff & Butter 
1984a). Butter and his colleague Krippendorff (1984b) argued that if the designer were 
aware of exactly how the product was working, he would be able to demystify complex 
technology and in that way optimize the interaction between the end-user and the product 
(Krippendorff & Butter 1984b). If product semantics are properly applied to the product the 
end-user will feel more psychologically comfortable and be able to connect emotionally to 
the otherwise impersonal product (Demirbilek & Sener 2003). The semantics can be 
expressive shapes or details that in some way expresses what the product wants to tell. This 
is one of the most important aspects when wanting end-users to interact with the product in a 
specific way. 
 
Norman calls the relationship between the controls and their actions mapping. The mapping 
is how you imagine the system inside the head. When you look at the switches in you living 
room you have a map in you head telling you which switch turns on which electronic device. 
Mapping differs from one culture to another, because of education and cultural differences 
(Norman 2013). This principle connects closely to the concept model and is like vice 
important for how the interaction between end-user and product proceed, because the 
mapping in the end-users mind has to fit the one in the engineers mind.  
 
==PAGE:22==
 
16 
A good design should provide constraints physically, logically, semantically, and culturally, 
thereby it eases interpretation and guides actions (Norman 2013). A good design shouldn’t 
contain more than necessary, so that unnecessary elements aren’t confusing the actor 
(Norman 2013). Norman argues that constraints ease the use because the end-user will not 
feel uncertain in the interaction with the product.  
 
Norman (2013) explains that the reason to a poor design can arise from one or more of the 
principles and that failure in the principles can result in poor design, but that it also might be 
caused by bad communication. Common for all of Norman’s principles are that they focus 
on the interaction between end-user and product. There is though no mentioning about any 
societal perspectives. 
 
2.2.3.2$Comparing$other$guidelines$
To get a better view of what guidelines can contribute to, Norman’s guidelines has been 
compared to guidelines from other fields. The comparison can be seen in appendix 1.   
The designer Dieter Rams has made 10 design rules to result in a good design (Vitsæ 2015). 
Rams takes as an industrial designer among others an aesthetic perspective. His rules are 
politically correct in the way that the product should be environmentally friendly and 
understandable. The rules don’t say much about how to obtain that on the other hand. Rams 
shares some rules/principals with Norman and many of the others that have developed design 
guidelines. It shows that even if they have made the principles for their own fields, the 
important aspects to consider when designing good product are still the same. 
Ben Shneiderman (2015) and Jacob Nielsen (1994) have developed two of the best-known 
sets of principles in the design of interfaces. They both have made similar lists of design 
principles, when trying to design a good interface. These lists are of cause different from 
Rams’ and Norman’s because it calls for another behaviour from the actor, when using 
interfaces. According to both Shneiderman (2015) and Nielsen (1994) it is important for a 
good design that it is possible to go back without any los. Also it is important that the design 
is consistent, so that the user isn’t confused during the use of the product. Shneiderman 
(2015) have a rule saying that it should be possible to se how far in the process you have 
come. Other than that they are very similar to the principals that Norman (2013) has set up. 
The principles give a set of instructions on what to consider when designing good products. 
==PAGE:23==
 
17 
They are all very general, which shows that the guidelines can be used at any product 
generally speaking.  
 
The guidelines have furthermore in common that they all explicitly consider the interaction 
between the end-user and the product. They show a recipe on how to obtain this interaction 
the best way and can be used by people from different fields. One thing that all of the 
guidelines are missing is though how to handle social interests. 
 
2.2.4 MAIN POINTS 
• All models from section 2.2 are primarily addressed to the company without any 
direct involvement from externs.  
• The QFD and GPEG both include the end-users interests at some level, but user 
involvement isn’t mandatory. 
• Storytelling design and scenario-based design both considers the end-user, but the 
company would have take initiative to go make field study.  
• The Kano-model, Intuitive model and Jacob Nielsen’s five defining elements are 
explanations on how the end-user ‘works’ and aren’t including any kind of end-
user interests.  
• The design principles all focus on the end-user and product interaction, though 
still without involving any societal perspective. 
 
This section has shown which types of models the engineers can use in a product 
development process. They have focused on the process and what to do when designing a 
good product. None of the models though explains how to change the interaction between 
the end-user and the product and how the engineers specifically should use this in a 
development process. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:24==
 
18 
2.3 COMPANY-USER INTERACTION 
As described in section 2.2, there exist a lot of methods, models and explanations that 
primarily includes the company. This section will look into methods focusing on the 
relationship between the company and the end-user and how these are involved.  
A selection of representative models will be explained in this section. Like for the company 
specific models there will be explained three types of models. 
 
• Behaviour change models are work methods that guide the designer through the 
process of behaviour change design from beginning to end. 
• Behaviour based models are strategies designed as tools to be used as a supplement 
along side other design tools. 
• Explain behaviour models are ways to explain behaviour changes, but the models 
don’t suggest how to include it in a developing process. 
 
2.3.1 BEHAVIOUR CHANGE METHODS 
The company specific methods all focused on how to fulfil the end-user’s need. The 
company-user interaction methods are also trying to fulfil the end-user’s needs, but focusing 
more on the end-users behaviour. 
 
2.3.1.1$Fogg’s$8$Step$Method$
B. J. Fogg has developed a method to change the end-user’s behaviour. This means that the 
interaction between end-user and product is in focus in the model. The model includes 8 
steps and builds upon persuasive design (Fogg 2009). B. J. Fogg is also the founder of the 
design discipline persuasive design (Tanggaard 2010).  
He starts by explaining that the greatest chance for success is if the change is small. Rather 
more small changes than one big (Fogg 2009). The first couple of steps in the 8 step method 
focuses on defining the problem or behaviour to change and defining the end-users influence. 
When the fundamentals are in order there have to be made observations to find out exactly 
what is creating the problems. The solutions are created from known examples that originate 
from persuasive technology. This implies that the use of already known results will ease the 
work of the development. The successful solutions are iterated to discover the best way to 
==PAGE:25==
 
19 
solve the problem. The last step of the solution making is to test the effect of the solution in 
the real life context. If the test result isn’t satisfying, the designer will have to go back in the 
process to iterate a new solution (Fogg 2009). When a final solution has been chosen the last 
step of the 8 step process can be started. This is to expand the success to other fields.  
The 8 step model shows a strong consideration about end-user and product interaction due to 
the observation and the testing of products. There is though no part of the model that 
includes societal interests.  
 
2.3.1.2$BASIS$Model$
At the nudge company iNudgeyou they use a similar kind of method when solving problems 
for companies like Copenhagen Municipality, Copenhagen Airport and Maersk. This model 
isn’t based on persuasive technology as the 8 step model, but is based on nudging (Hansen 
2014). The method is called BASIS and is an acronym for the steps guiding the designer 
through the process. The acronym makes it easy to remember the steps in a development 
process.  
The BASIS method starts by doing behavioural mapping. This includes observation at the 
location where the problem is, to specify what the exact problem is. The behavioural 
mapping is all about the end-user – product interaction and the observations include both 
conscious and unconscious end-user behaviour. From the behavioural mapping an analysis is 
done to create hypothesis on what the real problem is. The hypothesis forms the basis for the 
solution mappings. The solutions are found through nudging and behavioural science 
(nudging will be explained in section 2.3.1.5). As well as for the 8 step model, the BASIS 
model also uses known examples to ease the work. When a solution is chosen it is being 
tested at the original location. The testing is build upon an experience design, which is 
securing a valid result of the test (Hansen 2014). The test verifies if the interaction between 
the end-user and the product has changed in the expected way. Iterations may have to be 
conducted if the result isn’t satisfying. When a solution shows good results it is being 
expanded further. This model is similar to the 8 step model, but doesn’t focus on finding the 
right end-users or the right little problem. BASIS is a model that is used in real life and the 
company always gets the assignment including what problem to solve and which end-user it 
concerns.   
 
==PAGE:26==
 
20 
2.3.1.3$Behaviour$Design$Model$
Two engineering students have developed the Behaviour Design Model (BDM) as a master’s 
thesis project that examine how to use nudging theory in an operational design process 
(Durazo and Hartlev 2015). This brings the interaction between end-user and product into the 
development process thinking. The engineering background gives the model another 
foundation than the other two models and this brings it closer to the aim of this thesis.  
BDM starts like the other two methods with a behavioural mapping. But instead of doing the 
behavioural mapping in the field only, this method includes desk research as well. This gives 
a fundament for knowing more about the behaviour beforehand and opens up for a more 
informative observation. When the behavioural mapping is done a scoping phase takes place 
to define the problem. This leads to the development phase where solutions are created. This 
model doesn’t mention that earlier examples should be used to ease the development. The 
last step is a testing phase. Like the other two methods it is recommended to iterate if the 
result of the test isn’t satisfying (Durazo and Hartlev 2015).  
 
2.3.1.4$Comparison$of$the$behaviour$change$methods$$
Even though the three models are generated from three different fields they still include more 
or less the same steps. The essence of the three methods shows that there is a need for 
behaviour mapping in one or another way. iNudgeyou makes observations and makes the 
behaviour mapping from these observations. Fogg (2009) doesn’t say anything about how to 
make the behavioural mapping, just that a target behaviour and a receptive audience has to 
be chosen. Durazo and Hartlev (2015) combines these two starting with desk research and 
then field study. It gives three different starting points, but it is still the same they are looking 
for. The behavioural mapping differs from the company specific models, by building the 
project on a foundation of observations and thereby the interaction between end-user and 
product. This brings these models closer to the aim of the project.  
The solution mapping and testing are more or less the same for all three methods and 
supports the perception of the importance of end-user – product interaction.  
 
A visual exposition of the comparison of the three methods can be seen in appendix 3. 
 
In these types of methods the company-user interaction is central and the focus is on the 
interaction between end-user and product. It is essential to observe the end-user in the 
==PAGE:27==
 
21 
specific situation when doing behaviour changing design (Durazo and Charlotte 2015). The 
society perspective is scarce in these types of methods. If the municipality place an order for 
a behaviour change it might involve societal goals, but the models itself doesn’t include 
societal wishes. The BASIS model and the BDM model both uses nudging as a foundation 
and it is therefore interesting to look into the field of nudging, because this might be an 
important tool if the societal perspective should be included as well. 
 
2.3.1.5$Nudging$
Thaler and Sunstein (2008), who are the founders of the term nudge argues that as long as 
people are not choosing perfectly, some changes in the choice architecture can make peoples 
lives go better. If changes can be made in a choosing or an interaction between end-user and 
product, this will give an opportunity to make changes to encourage social interests. The 
concept is therefore explained underneath. 
 
The two systems 
Nudging builds upon two ways of thinking. Thaler and Sunstein (2008) askes how people 
can be simultaneously so smart and so dumb? An engineer can solve complex mathematical 
equations, but it wouldn’t be surprising to hear that he always misplaces his house key 
(Thaler & Sunstein 2008). The thing is that the human have two ways of thinking - one that 
is automatic and intuitive and one that is reflective and rational (Thaler and Sunsein, 2008). 
The automatic system is the one that makes us act like Homer Simpson (character from the 
animated series The Simpsons) – uncontrolled, effortless, associative, fast, unconscious and 
skilled (Kahneman 2011, p. 29, Norman 2013, p. 49, Thaler & Sunstein 2008, p. 22). The 
other one is the reflective system that are hyper rational and represents a Mr. Spock 
(character from Star Trek) way of thinking – controlled, effortful, deductive, slow, self-
aware and rule-following (Thaler and Sunstein, 2008). A very famous test that shows the 
automatic system in action - that is also described by Thaler and Sunstein (2008) – are a 
mathematical calculation with a ball and a bat. Try it! A bat and a ball cost $1,10 in total. 
The bat costs $1,00 more than the ball. How much does the ball cost? (Kahneman 2011, p. 
55, Thaler & Sunstein 2008, p. 23). Most people answers 10 cent. This is the automatic 
system in action. 10 cent is what first comes into mind – the answer is instinctively and fast – 
and wrong. If the reflective system had been used, it would have demanded more time to 
answer and more brain capacity. The right answer is 5 cent for the ball and 1,05 cent for the 
==PAGE:28==
 
22 
bat. The two ways of thinking explains how the human mind isn’t always rational and 
thereby can be changed. The end-user mind could for instance be changed to interact in a 
certain way with a specific product. Given that the end-users behaviour can be changed in a 
specific way, it brings up speculations about if the end-users interest is considered in the 
definition as well. 
 
Definition of nudge 
It could seem that a nudge could be manipulation, but Richard Thaler and Cass Sunstein 
defines a nudge the following way; 
 
‘A nudge is any aspect of the choice architecture that alters people’s behaviour in a 
predictable way without forbidding any options or significantly changing their economic 
incentives.’ (Thaler & Sunstein 2008; p. 6)  
 
This means that removing the unhealthy snack isn’t a nudge, but moving it to a less 
noticeable shelf is a nudge (Thaler & Sunstein 2008). This means that nudging isn’t 
manipulation, but just making another choice possible for the end-user. It is though still the 
end-user that has to make the free choice.  
Hansen (2016) sets up some rules for what a nudge is and what is isn’t to make it easier to 
recognize a nudge:  
A nudge is a function of (I) any attempt at influencing people’s judgment, choice or 
behaviour in a predictable way. Nudges are (1) made possible because of cognitive biases, 
routines and habits in individual and social decision-making posing barriers for people to 
perform rationally in their own interests, and (2) work by making use of those biases, 
routines and habits as integral parts of such attempts.  
Thus nudges amongst other things work independently of:  
! 
(i)  forbidding or adding any rationally relevant choice options,   
! 
(ii)  changing incentives, whether  regarded in terms of time, trouble, social sanctions,  
economic  and so forth, or   
! 
(iii)  the provision of factual information and rational argumentation.   
==PAGE:29==
 
23 
These rules can be used to determine whether it is a nudge or not. The following examples 
can help clarify this further.   
 
Examples of nudges 
All kind of nudges is some kind of choice that the actor is given and that tries to change the 
actor’s behaviour (Hansen 2016). Many examples of nudging that in some way have been 
connected to an object have been some kind of communication or visual sign that makes the 
actor change behaviour.  
 
• The fly in the urinal in Schiphol Airport (Thaler & Sunstein 2008) – a visual fly that 
getting the visitor to aim for the fly and thereby not hit beside the urinal.   
• The green trashcans in Copenhagen (iNudgeyou 2015) – the bins are light green, so that 
they are salient in the townscape, they are social salient (we think that what we focus on 
ourselves is also the focus for others (Thaler & Sunstein 2008)) which will make us ashamed 
if we do not hit the bin, and finally the green footprints that works as an instructions manual 
that leads us to the bin.  
 
==PAGE:30==
 
24 
• Lakeshore drive of Chicago (Thaler & Sunstein 2008) – the shore of Chicago is s-shaped 
and a lot of accidents caused by speeding occur every year. Then stripes were drawn across 
the road. First the distances between the stripes are the same, but as closer to the curve the 
driver comes the closer the stripes are together. This creates an illusion of increasing speed 
and makes the driver to slow down and thereby avoid an accident.  
 
All these kinds of nudges have the potential to change the behaviour of the end-user to the 
benefit of society. The behaviour change methods (sec 2.3.1) are all describing the 
development process from beginning to end and uses nudging as a fundament. 
Nudging has though also been used as a foundation for more problem oriented models. The 
models are based on behaviour with concrete suggestions on how to do. 
 
2.3.2 BEHAVIOUR BASED MODELS 
Behaviour based models can be used as a tool alongside other tools in a development 
process. Two of the best-known models are made by Artefact (Pfarr 2014) and Lockton et al 
(2010). These models are similar to the engineering Triz-model that tries to solve new 
problems by looking at similar known problems and using the known problem’s solutions 
(Cross 2000).  
 
2.3.2.1$Behaviour$Change$Strategy$Cards$
Behaviour Change Strategy Cards are a deck of cards inspired by work from the fields of 
behavioural economics and cognitive psychology. The deck is developed by Artefact who is 
a design company that has developed these cards to make behaviour changes easier (Pfarr 
==PAGE:31==
 
25 
2014). They seek to turn the insights from theses fields into knowledge that can be 
incorporated into a development process and a creative design. The argument for developing 
the card deck is that right now the insights from the behavioural economics and cognitive 
psychology fields are mainly communicated through academic papers and conference 
proceedings and thereby is hard to make use of in a development process (Pfarr 2014).  
The deck consists of 23 cards that should be used as a starting point to think through 
strategies and brainstorm new ideas to nudge people in a desirable behavioural direction. 
This is a more concrete way to include behaviour changes in the interaction between end-
user and product. Because it is this concrete it might be easier to use in an engineering 
development process. As well as for the 8 step model (Fogg 2009), persuasive design is a 
fundament for the cards. Artefact calls attention to the fact that behaviour changes don’t 
necessarily may result in long-term success even that they seem to be successful in a short-
term perspective. They highlight at the end of the description that; ’At the end of the day, the 
only way to make sure that what you’re designing has the outcome you desire is to test it 
with real people.’ (Pfarr 2014). This indicates that even that the cards are made on a 
foundation of behaviour change, it is still necessary to go out and do field research to gain 
the optimal product. 
 
2.3.2.2$Design$with$Intent$$$$
Another model also using a more concrete approach is Design with Intent developed by 
Lockton et al (2010). This model is a collection of patterns for influencing behaviour through 
design. The patterns or lenses are divided into eight categories including among others a 
perceptual lens, a cognitive lens and an architectural lens. Each lens has an introduction that 
explains to the user, which research fields this lens draws on. The cognitive lens for instance 
draws on behavioural economics and cognitive psychology. It looks at how people make 
decisions and how they are influenced by heuristics and biases. Lockton et al (2010) argues 
in this introduction that if designers understand this underlying research they can counter this 
in their design and in that way help end-users make better decisions. If the societal 
perspective were included in the cards, this would be a way to include both the end-user and  
the societal interests in the user phase.  
Each lens contains 10 to 17 examples on how to incorporate the underlying research in 
designs. An example from the cognitive lens could be ‘Social Proof’. Social proof is a 
behavioural insight and can be described by the phenomenon where people assume that 
==PAGE:32==
 
26 
others know better and what others do in a specific situation is the right behaviour (Cialdini 
1999). Each of the examples contains an overall question that can be used as inspiration for 
the designer. For ‘Social Proof’ it is ‘Can you show people what other users like them are 
doing in this situation, and which choices are most popular?’(Lockton et al 2010, p. 83). 
This tool could also be used to include societal goals in the user phase. On each card there is 
furthermore a concrete example on how it is used. In ‘Social Proof’ the concrete example is 
on how users of Amazone.com is being told what other customers has looked at, when they 
have bought a particular book. This will make the customers buy more.  
 
The two models above (sec. 2.3.2.1 and sec. 2.3.2.2) build on the behaviour of end-users and 
are presenting examples on how to use academic research in a development process.  
In regard to the interaction between society and the company, the models do not focus on 
including societal interests. In the Design with Intent model societal interest is ‘accidentally’ 
included in some of the examples, but there are no patterns or deliberate use. The same is 
valid for the Behaviour Change Strategy Cards. Given that the problem to solve was among 
the examples on the cards or similar to, these two methods would be a good way of solving 
problems. If the problem on the other hand weren’t one of the examples it would be 
necessary to know a little more about the behaviour behind.  
 
2.3.3 EXPLAIN BEHAVIOUR 
The last way of focusing on company/user interaction is ‘explain behaviour’-models. This 
kind of models can be used at any behaviour and explains what happens when an end-user 
behave in a certain way. Norman (2013) and Fogg (2012) have developed the two models  
explained in this section.  
 
2.3.3.1$The$Behaviour$Grid 
B. J. Fogg (2012) has developed a Behaviour Grid to make developers think more clearly 
about behaviour changes. The model is built from knowledge about psychology and 
persuasive techniques. The Behaviour Grid is, like the name insinuate, shaped as a grid or a 
matrix. In the top row each box represents an action according to a behaviour change. It 
could be ‘do new behaviour, one that is unfamiliar’ or ‘stop doing a behaviour’ (Fogg 2012). 
The boxes in the left column, represents different amounts of times for behaviour. E.g. ‘…is 
done one-time’ or ‘…is done from now on, a permanent change’ (Fogg 2012). When 
==PAGE:33==
 
27 
crossing the top row boxes with the left column boxes different behaviour changes are 
enlightened. E.g. the ‘stop doing a behaviour’ and ‘…is done from now on, a permanent 
change’ could be never litter again. This model explains behaviour, but demands a lot more  
thinking from the developer than the behaviour based models. 
 
2.3.3.2$Seven$Stages$Of$Action 
The second model to explain behaviour is developed by Norman (2013) and is referred to as 
the Seven Stages of Action in his book The Design of Every Day Things. The seven steps are 
providing a checklist of questions to ask when doing design. According to Norman any user 
should always be able to answer all seven questions and it is the designers responsibility that 
it is possible to do. He defines the information that helps answering the questions for doing 
an action as feedforward and information that helps understand the action feedback (Norman 
2013). It is from this model that the seven design principles mentioned in section 2.2.3 is 
emerged. The Seven Stages of Action can, according to Norman (2013), both enlighten why 
design is good or why design is bad seen from the end-users perspective.  
 
The Behaviour Grid works as a ‘before developing’ explanation model. It helps determine 
what the designer or engineer should consider according to behaviour when develop the new 
products. The Seven Stages of Action model is more of an ‘after developing’ checklist 
model. This model can be used to answer if the design is working intentionally or why an 
already existing product is a bad design. Both models are explanation models and don’t 
involve the end-user or society unless the company approach it to do this. The end-user – 
products 
interaction 
is 
though 
essential 
to 
both 
the 
models. 
 
2.3.4 MAIN POINTS  
• The behaviour change models all focus on the end-user – product interaction. This 
is done by observing the end-user in the user phase and thereby get a good 
understanding of what problems could appear in the interaction. The societal 
perspective isn’t considered.  
• The field of nudging is using knowledge from behavioural economics and choice 
architecture to change the end-users behaviour. Nudging is a tool that easily could 
be taken into account when wanting products that both take end-user and societal 
interests into account.  
==PAGE:34==
 
28 
• Behaviour based models can be used alongside other design tools. They build on 
academic research on behaviour, but presents a much more concrete way of 
including behaviour change in a developing process. They are though hard to use 
if the problem isn’t among the once explained in the cards. 
• Explain behaviour models can be used as check lists and aren’t suggesting how to 
do behaviour changes. The end-user – product interaction is in focus, but there is 
no mentioning of society in these models.  
 
The models explained so far have included the company’s interests and the end-users 
interests. They have also been focusing on the end-user – product interaction and how to 
change this interaction in the user phase behaviour. There has though not been any 
mentioning about the social interests and how to consider these in a development phase. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:35==
 
29 
2.4 SOCIETY PERSPECTIVES AND INTERACTIONS 
The last actor to consider is Society. They also have goals that they want fulfilled.  
Society can be explained by the total of the end-users and thereby the societal interests will 
be the overall interest of the total of end-users. Figure 2 is a showing of that the individual 
and the collective differs and thereby have different interests. 
 
 
Figure 2 - Society consists of end-users 
 
Given that there is a difference between individual and collective interests the 
collective/societal interests would have to be clarified to include those in a development 
process. 
 
2.4.1 SOCIETAL GOALS 
Society desires many different wishes, such as decrease in pollution and increased welfare to 
the population. As this project is focussing on product development this section will only 
look at what society wants according to physical products.  
Society has set up different groups of people to determine what the common interest is. One 
of the most influential is The United Nations. 
 
2.4.1.1$The$United$Nations$Development$Program$
The United Nations Development Program (UNDP) has set up some goals that they want to 
achieve by the year 2030 (UNDP 2015). The three overall categories are 1) social progress, 
2) economic growth, and 3) environmental protection (UNDP 2015). These overall 
categories have been expanded to 17 goals in the Sustainable Development Goals plan (app.

SOCIETY
==PAGE:36==
 
30 
4). Under each goal some targets have been specified in detail (SDG 2015). Many of the 
targets can’t have any influence on product design or product development, unless the 
product is designed to fulfil a specific target. E.g. by 2030, achieve universal and equitable 
access to safe and affordable drinking water for all (SDG 2015). This can’t be used in 
product development in general, but would be fulfilled if the designer would make a device 
providing fresh water for people in Africa. This example shows that societal goals also are 
dependent on the country’s state of development and cultural background. This thesis will 
focus on the social goals for developed countries and especially the Danish population. 
Several of the targets in the UNPD are addressed to the developing countries, because of the 
lack of achieved goals in these countries. The UNDP primarily tells how the lower needs of a 
product Maslow’s pyramid should be fulfilled, while this project deals with how to develop 
good design that probably would belong in the upper end of the pyramid (Poston 2009).   
When looking through the development program and only considering what can be used as 
product development criteria, 4 areas leaps out. 
   
• Safety for the user 
o Mechanical safe (Concerns user and company) 
o No toxic materials (Concerns user) 
• Environmentally Friendly 
o Sustainability in all the phases of the product life (Concerns company) 
o Reusable or recyclable materials (Concerns Company and user) 
o User awareness of sustainable use and disposal (Concerns user and company) 
• Energy-saving 
o Sustainable energy (Concerns user)  
o Energy-saving possibilities (Concerns company and user) 
• Save money 
o Decrease possibility of failure  
 
Safety for the user 
First goal is to make safe products. In 2013 41.362 work accidents were reported to the 
Danish Working Environment Authority (Arbejdstilsynet 2014). A number of these were 
related to unintended use of products. If the product had communicated right some of these 
accidents could have been avoided. 
==PAGE:37==
 
31 
Not only is the society interested of having mechanical safe products they also want products 
that aren’t toxic. E.g. The European Union has published a list containing all the substances 
that can disrupt human hormones (Miljøstyrelsen 2016). Among these are phthalates that has 
been used in plastic toys for children. If a product is save both mechanical and chemical, 
fewer people will get sick and the society will save money.   
 
Environmentally friendly and energy-saving     
The second group of goals is to protect the environment. A product should be sustainable in 
all it’s life phases: extraction of raw material, manufacturing, distribution, use and disposal 
(Owen 1997). The first phases are mainly the company’s responsibility. …Incorporating 
production processes, the products themselves and the behaviour they trigger within the 
limits of ecological design (Barero & Brunella 2009, p. 10). In the user phase and the 
disposal phase the design should lead the user to act as intended, because it in the end is the 
user who decides what to do. This means that the product design is crucial for obtaining the 
intended interaction between end-user and product. 
The two goals environmentally friendliness and Energy-saving are closely related. For 
instance is CO2 emission a part of both. To have a sustainable product lifecycle the CO2 
emission would have to be reduced and sustainable energy is all about reducing CO2 
emission. The Danish government has set a goal that by 2020 the Danish society should 
decrease CO2 emission by 40 % (according to the reference year 1990) (Klima- Energi- og 
Bygningsministeriet 2013). In 2050 the emission of CO2 should be decreased by 80-95 % 
compared to 1990 (Klima- Energi- og Bygningsministeriet 2013). This Danish governmental 
goal substantiates both the second and third group of goals set up by The United Nations. 
Given that the Danish goals are the same as The United Nations, these are considered valid 
for this project. 
  
Save money 
The last of the four goals is to save money. This is hard to say when and where, but it could 
be products that decrease possibility of failure as for instance trashcans in the townscape. 
Every year Danish municipalities use great resources on handling of discarded waste and the 
expenses are increasing (Miljøministeriet 2008).  As mentioned in section 2.3.1.5 
Copenhagen Municipality have used nudging as a method to get the citizens to make use of 
the trashcans and thereby save money on cleaning the streets. 
 
==PAGE:38==
 
32 
It has now been showed that The United Nations Development Program also is relevant for 
Denmark and thereby also for other similar countries. These goals can directly be used in 
product development, but isn’t forced upon the companies.  
A current way the companies show social responsibility is to create a CSR strategy. 
 
2.4.1.2$CSR$Strategies$
CSR outlines some very concrete statements on what the company should do and abstain 
from to be social responsible. The ISO standard 26000 and Global Reporting Initiative G4 
both have made guidelines on how to make a CSR strategy (ISO and GRI 2014). Three 
representative examples from these lists could be: climate change mitigation and adaptation, 
health and safety at work, and promoting social responsibility in the value chain. Common 
for all the guiding statements are that none of them give any concrete guide on how to obtain 
the statement and many of the guidelines are directed to the company and how they can 
change to be more social responsible. To have a CSR-strategy is not a guarantee for being 
social responsible (ISO and GRI 2014). Neither is it a guarantee for obtaining intended use in 
between the end-user and the product in the user phase that takes societal interests into 
account. 
The way society currently makes sure that their interests are taken into account is by laws 
and regulations.  
 
2.4.1.3$Laws$and$Regulations$
The government makes rules for the companies to follow to make sure that the environment 
and the other goals are taken into account. The users also have to follow some rules to ensure 
that they also take these interests into account. It isn’t though sure that laws always help 
obtaining the social goal.  
E.g. in 2009 EU enacted a law that production and distribution of the incandescent bulb 
should be prohibited (Jensen 2009). The incandescent bulbs should be phased out and be 
totally replaced by the fluorescent light bulb and some halogen bulbs by 2012 (Jensen 2009). 
This law should help EU save 15 million tons of CO2 emission to the atmosphere per year 
(Jensen 2009). Unfortunately this can backfire due to the ‘rebound effect’. This phenomenon 
is when consumers buy environmentally friendly products and then increase the use resulting 
in the same or worth environmentally burden than the conventional product (Herring & Roy 
==PAGE:39==
 
33 
2007). Does an actor install a fluorescent light bulb instead of an incandescent bulbs, he 
might leave the light on because he thinks that he is environmentally safe, but in the end this 
turns out to be worth than the conventionally bulb (Herring & Roy 2007). This is an example 
on how the government tries to legislate to better environment, but without real life effect. 
The example also shows that laws don’t necessarily always help the way they were intended. 
So even that society tries to influence on the company is isn’t enough to influence the 
interaction between end-user and product. Therefor another way has to be found to include 
the societal interests. 
 
2.4.2 SOCIETY AND THE COMPANY AND THE USER 
The amounts of society representing models directed to companies are negligible small. As 
sections 2.4.1 above alludes to, the relation between society and a company mainly goes one-
way. Society sets up standards, laws and regulation that the company has to obey to avoid 
tickets. The users are also forced to obey the laws if they want to avoid tickets. The 
relationship between the end-user and society isn’t thereby no better than the relationship 
between the company and society. 
 
2.4.2.1$End?user$and$society$relations$
Tromp et al (2011) has developed a model that tries to enlighten the relationship between 
society and the end-user. The model distinguishes between the individual and collective 
concerns (comparable to what this thesis calls end-user interests and societal interests). It sets 
focus on the fact that collective concerns and individual concerns are not always the same. 
Figure 3 shows the visual illustration on this correlation. 
 
==PAGE:40==
 
34 
 
Figure 3 - Tromp et al's model (2011) – The framework figure 
 
The next four examples are going to show why the collective and individual concerns are not 
the same in four different ways and underpin that rules not always are the best way to go.  
 
Society influences the end-user 
In New York overpasses over the parkway on Long Island obstruct busses to drive under 
because of the height of the overpasses. This means that the beach ‘Jones Beach’ only is 
available for car owners and not people who make use of public transportation (Winner 
1980). This example shows how product design can force people to behave in a curtain way 
and give preferential treatment to some people. Society can here through a company 
influence the end-user in a specific way and thereby take some control over the users without 
using laws and regulations.  
 
Same goal for different reasons 
The next example is showing how social and end-user goals are the same, but for two very 
different reasons. Speed bumps are made by society to make drivers slow down. The drivers 
slow down, but not because of the presence of the speed bump. They slow down, because 
they don’t want to damage their car (Latour 1992). This example shows that societies 
intentions not always are followed for the right reasons. This brings to attention the 
importance of distinguishes between the individual and the collective concerns.

MEANS
GOAL

influencing

==PAGE:41==
 
35 
Changing end-user habits unintended 
The third example shows how the users behaviour can change without any intended 
wirepuller. A microwave oven is often used to heat ready-made meals and because of this 
easy way to make dinner, more families have started to eat more separate (Verbeek 2005). 
The microwave oven has contributed to an unintentional behaviour pattern. Verbeek 
illustrates in this example how a product actively influence on the relation between the end-
user and the surroundings.   
 
Changing the product to change habit 
The last example shows how the plate size influence on how much food we eat. Brian 
Wansink (2004) has conducted an experiment showing that the size of a plate matches the 
amount of food that we take and thereby the amount of food that we eat. This is explained by 
an automatic response where people seems to match the amount of food to the plate size and 
don’t think about how much they can eat. In public spaces it might also be because overload 
of the plate might feel embarrassing (Wansink 2004).  
The plate example doesn´t address societal concerns directly, but by triggering the end-user, 
the social goal to avoid obese citizens and lesser food waste is reached anyway. This means 
that if the company is aware of the relationship between the product and the end-user in the 
development phase, they might be able to change the end-user’s behaviour and in 
collaboration with societal goals this might be the key to a product that will fulfil all actors’ 
needs.  
 
The four examples show how design affects behaviour in both intended and unintended 
ways. It also shows that it is possible to influence the users behaviour without using 
standards, laws and regulation. Society wouldn’t be able to make new products itself, but in 
collaboration with a product development company that would take society goals into 
consideration when developing new products, it would be possible to realise society goals. 
Tromp et al (2011) argues that the power of design lies in the potential of bridging the end-
user concerns with society concerns. An obvious actor to make a bridge is the engineer, 
while they already now a lot about how to fulfil the end-users needs and develop products 
and then ‘just’ would have to incorporate societal needs into a holistic solution. The 
engineers would just have to be convinced to do consider societal goals in the development 
process. 
 
==PAGE:42==
 
36 
2.4.2.2$Include$societal$goals$in$engineering$development$
Tromp et al (2011) argues that there are 2 reasons why designers/companies would start 
designing for society.  
 
1. The designers are behaving more social responsible in the product development 
phase – the company would have to take initiative themselves. 
2. Designers apply design methodology and design thinking to social issues – the 
company cases would be social issues and not regular products like a car or a 
microwave oven. 
 
To be able to use the most effective method or strategy, the relationship between the end-
user concerns and societal concerns has to be identified (Tromp et al 2011). When the 
concerns are identified Tromp et al (2011) suggests two ways for the designer to change end-
user behaviour.  
Designers can use either encouragement or discouragement to try to change behaviour.  
 
Discouragement 
The London tube stations are provided with ticket portals and you can only go through the 
portals if you have a valid ticket (Tromp et al 2011). These ticket portals are designed to 
discourage undesired behaviour. This works for the travellers that normally would forget to 
buy a ticket, but not for those who normally wouldn’t pay for the ticket and can still jump the 
portal.  
 
Encouragement 
In South Korea the subway tickets are both a ticket to the train, but also a ticket to a lottery, 
that you can participate in if you buy a ticket.  
The lottery ticket is designed to encourage desired behaviour. This way of designing the 
ticket system gets the fare dodgers and the people that enjoy taking risks to buy tickets.  
These two examples show that knowledge about the underlying reasons for not doing a 
specific behaviour is important. 
Tromp et al (2011) argues that a design can influence the end-user on two different axis; 
force and salience. The force is how much the product exerts influence – it can go from weak 
to strong. A product can be salient in an implicit or a more explicit way. From these two axis 
==PAGE:43==
 
37 
four different dimensions on how to influence end-users appears: coercive, persuasive, 
seductive and decisive. From this Tromp et al (2011) has developed a model where different 
designs can be placed to explain their influence on the user. 
 
 
Figure 4 - Tromp et al's figure (2011) – Four types of influences 
 
A product can’t define a category only the human interacting with the product can determine 
whether it is coercive, persuasive, seductive and decisive. It will also vary from person to 
person and from one context to another. 
Tromp et al (2011) concludes that ‘Designers no longer can hide behind the needs and 
wishes of the consumer; instead, they have to take responsibility as “shapers” of society’. 
Given that the societal perspective becomes more and more important it means that there is a 
need for a model to help engineers to take societal goals as well as user needs into 
consideration, besides standards, laws and regulation. ‘The individual does not always 
embrace or prioritize collective concerns’ (Tromp et al 2011, p. 7).  
 
2.4.3 MAIN POINTS 
• The societal goals are dependent on the country’s state of development and cultural 
background. 
• If the product communicates right some accidents can be avoided and fewer people 
will get sick and the society will thereby save money. This means that the product 
design is crucial for obtaining the intended interaction between end-user and product. 
• The current way for society to make sure that their interests are taken into account is 
by laws and regulations. Law doesn’t though always helps obtaining the social goal.  
• The power of design lies in the potential of bridging the end-user concerns with 
society concerns. To be able to use the most effective method or strategy, the

relationship between the end-user concerns and societal concerns has to be identified. 
Both the bridging and the identification of end-user and societal concerns should be 
made by the engineer, because he knows the end-user needs beforehand.   
• To work with social concerns or interests the engineer would be needing a model to 
help take societal goals as well as end-user needs into consideration, besides 
standards, laws and regulation. 
The literature review has now shown that there is a gap in the models where all of the  
actor’s interests are included. Figure 5 underneath shows visually where the gap is.
