The only model considering all three actors are the one by Tromp et al (2011), but this 
models doesn’t give any instructions on how an engineer should work to include both social 
and end-user interests in a development process.  
The next step of the thesis will be to determine a way to explore if the gap also exists in real 
world development. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:46==
 
40 
3. METHODOLOGY 
 
 
In the literature review (sec. 2) several different methods, explanation models, guidelines, 
standards and legislations have been mapped out to show the grouping and whose interests 
that is in focus. A gap emerges between the three actors as shown in figure 5. From this we 
see a problem because societal interests aren’t included in the interaction between end-user 
and product. The methodology is going to determine how to explore if the gap also exists in 
real world product development.   
 
3.1 INTERVIEW 
To find out if the gap also appears in real world product development there are 4 main 
questions that should be answered: 
 
1. Does the gap also exist in real world work? This is the overall question and will 
show if there is a need for a societal approach in engineering product development.  
 
2. What is the current situation between the company, the end-user and society? 
To know how the gap looks and how the different actors are included in the real 
world process this question would have to be answered. 
 
3. What could be the challenges in getting the company to count in societal goals? 
If a new model were to be incorporated in the development process, this question will 
help answering if there would be any resistance. 
 
4. Where in the development process would it be possible to include a new model? 
This question will show if it is at all possible to include a new method to close the 
gap. 
 
To answer these questions an approach has to be found that will give the best possible output 
as a foundation for the analysis. 
 
==PAGE:47==
 
41 
3.1.1 CHOSEN APPROACH 
To expose the situation of the current engineering development process interviews have been 
made with actors relevant due to the staring framework (fig. 1). Qualitative research 
interviews are an approach to get an understanding of the interviewee’s point of view, 
expand the meaning that is attached to their experiences and expose their view before doing 
scientific explanations (Kvale and Brinkman 2009). Interviews would thereby gain insight 
into the current development process and thereby it would be possible to answer the main 
questions. According to Harrits et al (2010) interviews are the best way of gathering data if 
one of the following three situations is current: 1) interest of view and meaning, 2) an 
explorative study, or 3) interest for unique information. To answer the main questions, it is 
relevant to understand the interests, perspectives and work field of the chosen actors to 
discover the actors view and meaning according to interaction, gathering knowledge about 
development processes and get unique information about the real world work. This means 
that all three situations are meet and interviews is thereby a good way of getting the main 
questions answered.  
Individual interviews have been chosen rather than group interviews. Individual interviews 
are well suited for access to specific and concrete information from central actors (Harrits et 
al 2010).  
 
3.1.2 INTERVIEWEES  
The choice of interviewees has been made on the basis of the actors included in the starting 
framework (fig. 1) and the main questions. 
 
Engineers  
To cover the engineering company’s point of view engineers have been chosen as one type 
of interviewee. The engineer’s has great knowledge about how the development of products 
is conducted in the company and which processes and methods that are used. The criteria for 
chosen interviewees in this category are that the interviewee would have to be a part of a 
developing team in an engineering company. The interviewees should furthermore be 
thoroughly versed in the development process. This will give the best requirements for 
answering questions about the development process. 
 
==PAGE:48==
 
42 
Society 
The societal perspective is hard to get, because it is an average perspective of all end-users 
and it isn’t possible to interview all end-users.  
Municipalities are closely connected to the end-users needs given that they have to ensure 
that society functions on a daily basis in the townscape. This project has a physical product 
perspective and therefor employees from the department of Technique and Environment 
(TE) in different municipalities has ben chosen to represent the societal perspective. The 
employees from TE also perform tasks including some degree of product development and 
can therefor give perspectives and information on development phases as well. This double 
role can also enlighten possible issues that may occur between the interests of the developer 
and society.  
The interviewees chosen in this category would have to thoroughly verse in the projects 
conducted in the municipality. Besides that they should also have knowledge about processes 
and decisions during the projects. Thereby these people will help answering how societal 
perspectives are involved in their processes and what interests they have according to 
development of new products. 
 
End-users 
The end-user is the last of the three actor groups in the starting framework (fig. 1). The end-
user has no influence on the development process unless the engineer includes them. This 
means that it would be possible to get the end-users point of view through the engineers.  
If the end-user would have to be included anyway it would call for countless interviews 
because of the subjective position that each end-user has to a product, plus the countless 
number of different products. If one product would be chosen as a case-product this would 
be to specific to answer more general questions and it might give an incorrect picture of the 
end-users situation. What is important for a car isn’t the same as what is important for a 
trashcan.  
 
As far as possible all interviewees are interviewed in their familiar surroundings, to ensure a 
safe feeling for the informant in an unaccustomed situation (Kvale and Brinkman 2009).  
The number of interviews is dependent upon saturation of information. Each interview will 
be performed through a semi structured interview guide and with only one informant at a 
time.  
==PAGE:49==
 
43 
All interviews are done in Danish, because all interviewees are Danish and working in 
Danish companies. This prevents that any language barrier would appear and thereby create 
an issue to the interviewees 
 
To find interviewees snowballing has been used as a recruitment method. This method draws 
on the network and knowledge of the interviewees. The interviewees will give suggestions 
on whom to talk to, to get more information about the specific area (Maaløe 2002). This way 
of gathering informants should give a broad view of the interests and processes that is 
important for this project.  
 
3.1.2.1$List$of$interviewees$

Troels Böwadt Arnved: Design engineer at Medicologic. Medicologic is doing medical 
device development. 
 
From the choosing of interviewees the interview guide could be made to answer the main 
questions. 
 
3.1.3 CONSTRUCTION OF INTERVIEW GUIDE 
There has been made two semi structured interview guides – one for the engineers and one 
for the municipality employees. Both of the interview guides are build on three main aspects:  
 
1. Introduction: The introduction questions are to get information about the specific 
company/municipality, how they work and what a typical assignment looks like. 
 
2. Development process: These questions are to get knowledge about how a typical 
development process is done, which considerations they have and which actors they 
include in the current process.  
 
3. Involvement of other actors and perspectives: The last aspect of questions is to 
find out if they consider societal goals at any level in the development process.    
 
Before starting the interview  
At the start of each interview guide there is a small exposition of the project and the purpose 
of the interview. There are no questions in the introduction. The interviewee has in advance 
==PAGE:51==
 
45 
given permission to recording of the interview. The recording is used to transcribe the 
interviews, so that these transcriptions can be used as foundation to the analysis.    
 
Introduction 
The purposes of the first questions has three main goals:  
 
• To get an overview of the kind of projects in the company/municipality. 
• To visualize the role of the interviewee in the development process.  
• To get an insight of the company structure and who they are collaborating with.  
 
These questions create a common fundament for both the interviewee and the interviewer to 
continue the interview. Furthermore they create a feeling of self-confidence for the 
interviewee, because these questions are easy to answer and get the flow going (Kvale and 
Brinkman 2009). These questions partly answer main question 2 (sec. 3.1) by giving an 
impression on how the stakeholder network looks in the real world and what influences each 
stakeholder has on one another.  
 
Development process 
The following questions are about the development processes. They focus on five elements 
to show how the process is conducted: 
 
• Elements in the development phase from beginning to end – e.g. field work. 
• If they use any kind of models in the development process. 
• If they have to follow certain demands from other stakeholders. 
• How they involve interests from other actors. 
• If they do any testing of the product in the development phase.  
 
These questions represent the main part of the interviews and will reveal the work processes. 
This part of the interview will reveal if there occur any gaps in the development process or 
contradictory elements that requests further elaboration and thereby answer main question 1 
(sec. 3.1).  
The interview guide is semi structured and can therefor be seen as a guide with suggestions 
on what questions to ask if the interviewee doesn’t tell naturally (Kvale and Brinkman 2009). 
==PAGE:52==
 
46 
It is not a strict manual to follow. It isn’t necessary to ask all questions or to ask the 
questions in the same order as they are written. It is possible to adjust the questions 
according to the interviewee, the projects and the situation. Semi structured interviews has 
better terms for the interviewer to improvise and follow up on hunches during the interview 
(Kvale and Brinkman 2009). 
This part of the interview will clarify the current situation on how the actors interact and 
show if and where it would be possible to implement a new model in the development 
process. Thereby the questions have contributed to answering main question 2 and 4 (sec. 
3.1). 
 
Involvement of other actors and perspectives 
The last questions are focussing on to what extend the company/municipality includes 
societal perspectives in the development of new products. The questions focus on three 
aspects of the societal perspective according to the findings in section 2.4.1:  
 
• Health and safety for the end-user using the product. 
• Environmentally challenges in the user phase. 
• Economical issues in the user phase. 
 
All questions in the interview guide are open-ended, because this increases the validity of the 
interviewees’ response. ‘Open-ended questions provide a greater opportunity for 
respondents to organize their answers within their own frameworks. This increases the 
validity of the responses and is best for the kind of exploratory and in-depth work (...)‘ 
(Aberbach and Rockman 2002, p. 674).  
These questions helps to answering main question 1, 2 and 3 (sec. 3.1), by clarifying if the 
gap exists, how the current satiation is between the actors and if there would be any 
implications in implementing a new model to the development process including societal 
goals. 
 
After the interview 
To wrap up the interview the interviewee is asked if he or she knows someone that could 
contribute to the information (snowballing) and if he or she would answer questions later in 
the process if any appears.  
==PAGE:53==
 
47 
 
The interview guides is shown in appendix 5 and 6. There is one interview guide showing an 
engineer interview and one showing a municipality employee interview. 
 
3.1.4 TRANSCRIPTION OF INTERVIEWS 
The transcriptions of the interviews aren’t done using the exact wording. According to Kvale 
and Brinkman (2009), there is no true objective transformation from the verbal interview to 
the written review. An adjustment of the transcription according to the study purpose can 
therefore be seen as an attempt to increase the validity. Only the elements from the interview, 
that are relevant to the project, have been transcribed and are going to be used in the data 
processing. The elements consists of descriptions of the general information about the 
company and the employee, specific descriptions of the project processes and descriptions of 
how the company includes end-user perspectives and societal perspectives. All of these 
descriptions aren’t written in direct wording. Besides that some quotations in direct wording, 
only what is relevant according to the aim of the project, has been written down in the 
interview reviews. This could for instance be Christian from Eupry (app. 7; q. 21) that says: 
‘Environmental impacts isn’t something we as such have had our focus on.’ The quotations 
are translated into English, when used in the thesis.  
Each of the transcriptions can be seen in appendix 7 to 16. 
 
3.1.5 DATA PROCESSING OF INTERVIEW REVIEWS 
Open coding is used to data the processing. This type of coding is about attaching key words 
and themes to pieces of text and in that way be able to identify the meaning later on (Miles 
and Huberman 1994). 
First all of the interviews have been given a colour, so that it is possible to recognise which 
interviewee that said what, when the information are divided into coding categories. The 
overall coding categories are made to help answering the main questions and builds upon the 
categories and questions used in the interview guide. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:54==
 
48 
Coding parameters 
Development process 
• Current work process, models or methods: this coding parameter covers information 
about how the current process is conducted and which models and methods that are 
used in the company and the municipality.  
 
• Interaction with the end-user to fulfil end-user needs: this coding parameter is 
covering information and quotations about how the company/municipality currently 
include the end-user in the development process to fulfil the end-user’s needs.  
 
• Inclusion of societal goals: this coding parameter gathers information and quotations 
about if it is possible to include societal goal guidelines and if so where in the process 
they should be included. This part of the coding has only been done for the company, 
because the result of the project should be addressed to the company. 
 
Involvement of other actors and perspectives 
• Forced societal approach: this coding parameter covers information and quotations 
about how the company/municipality are forced by law and regulations to take 
societal goals into account. 
 
• Voluntarily societal approach: this coding parameter cover information and 
quotations about if the company/municipality currently take social goals that aren’t in 
the shape of laws and regulations into account voluntarily.  
 
• Limitations to include societal goals: this coding parameter covers what reasons the 
company could have for not including societal goals. This is going to show what the 
guidelines are up against to be included in a development process. This part of the 
coding has only been done for the company, because the result of the project should 
be addressed to the company and it is thereby the limitations from the company that 
are relevant for this project. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:55==
 
49 
Comparison of company and municipality 
• Differences between company and municipality: this coding parameter is to 
determine what the differences between the company and the municipality are. 
Furthermore it is going to clarify if the municipality knowledge or the municipality 
process can be used to shape the result of the project. 
 
The coded interviews can be seen in appendix 17 and 18. 
After having divided the interview transcriptions into the coding categories, the categories 
have been distilled down to clarify the essence of the information. This part can be seen in 
appendix 19.  
The analysis (sec. 4) has been written on the basis of these coding parameters.  
 
 
3.2 FEEDBACK INTERVIEWS 
To validate the result of the project, two feedback interviews have been conducted. The two 
feedback interviews also help recovering what further work will require. 
The first of the feedback interviews was conducted with one of the engineers from the 
interviews done earlier in the project. He was selected to be an interviewee to this interview 
as well, because he already new a little about the project and thereby would be able to add 
more value to the validation.  
The feedback gained from the feedback interview with the engineer was used as a foundation 
for a feedback interview with the people from Copenhagen municipality. The second 
feedback interview was conducted with the employee from Copenhagen municipality that 
was interviewed to the first interviews and one of his co-workers. They both have 
engineering background and therefor they seemed to be competent to use as feedback 
interviewees, because of their knowledge about municipality obligations and on the same 
time engineering development. The result of the project is after all addressed to engineers. 
 
Introduction 
Each of the feedback interviews started with a presentation of the models found in the 
analysis (sec. 4) and duration of the result to show the findings and results so far. Thereby 
the interviewees got an understanding of which direction the project had taken and what they 
were going to give feedback on. 
 
==PAGE:56==
 
50 
Feedback questions 
As a foundation for the feedback three questions were made. These questions were made, as 
a semi structured interview guide to make sure that the interviewees would be guided to give 
feedback. The three main questions that should be answered to validate the result was: 
 
• Do you have any questions? – This will indicate if the result are easy 
understandable and if there is something that should be changed. 
• Would you be interested in using the result – why/why not? – This question will 
help getting the interviewee to se flaws and possible changes that could be counted 
into the guidelines to make them more suitable for the interviewee. 
• Would you add something to the result? – This question will help the interviewee 
to se possibilities and thereby be able to give suggestions on how to change the result 
to fit their situation. 
 
The feedback interviews were as well as the interviews earlier in the project made in Danish, 
because the interviewees are Danish and it thereby would avoid languish limitations. The 
interviews were transcribed similar to the other interviews (app. 20 and 21).  
The information gathered from the interview feedback is used to support parts of the 
discussion (sec. 5) and to write the further work (sec 6.1).  
 
 
==PAGE:57==
 
51 
4. ANALYSIS 
 
In this section the outcome of the interviews will be outlined. The sections are divided in 
three sub sections.  
First section 4.1 is the development process and will give an overall picture of the current 
situation for the municipality and the engineering company.  
Second section 4.2 is the involvement of other actors and perspectives and focus on how the 
municipality and the engineering company take societal perspectives into account.  
Third section 4.3 is about under which terms the municipality make projects and a 
comparison on how this differs from the engineering way of doing projects.  
 
All sub questions that has been answered can be found in the interview guides in appendix 5 
and 6. In the introduction to each category it will be outlined why the category has been 
chosen to include in the analysis. In the end of each category section there will be bullet 
points summing up the essence of the specific category. 
 
During the interviews and the information gathered from these, a new framework or 
stakeholder network appeared. This includes a customer company that often is involved as 
business partner or customer in the development process. Furthermore the product has been 
included, because it is the interaction between the end-user and the product that is important 
to this project.

A description of the names and companies mentioned during this section can be seen in 
section 3.1.2.1. 
 
4.1 DEVELOPMENT PROCESSES 
The reason for including questions about the development process and specific models in the 
interview guide was first of all to get an overview of the work processes of the municipalities 
and the companies (sec. 3). Furthermore it was to see which kind of models that are used in 
reality, and to see if one of these models could make an opening for including the 
perspective on societal goals. Finally it was a way to see how much the company and the 
municipality include the end-user interests, to see if the picture shaped in the literature 
review (sec. 2) was the same in reality. The first part investigates the municipality’s point of 
view and the second part investigates the engineering companies point of view. On overview 
and a description of the interviewees can be seen in the methodology section 3.1.2.1. 
 
4.1.1 THE MUNICIPANITY 
4.1.1.1$Current$work$process,$models$or$methods$
Work models 
It differs from municipality to municipality if they have an actual process work model that 
will guide them through the process. Søren (app. 15; q. 6) is the only one to mention an 
actual work model that they follow when doing projects. He didn’t though go into detail, but 
mentioned that they only used the model if it made sense. The rest of the municipalities 
didn’t have a work model to guide them through the developing process. This points to that 
municipalities doesn’t use work models like the ones described in the literature review (sec. 
2). 
The most of the municipalities though takes as its point of departure some kind of 
requirement specification. In Lyngby-Tårbæk municipality they don’t do many products 
themselves, but then they are the ones to make the requirement specification and give to the 
company that is doing/producing the product.  
==PAGE:59==
 
53 
This shows that the municipality plays more than one role. They can both be the developing 
company and the customer company. 
    
Methods 
The interviewees from the municipalities all make evaluations of the projects. It happens in 
different ways, by questionnaire, internal evaluation or measurements of the effects at the 
end of a project.  
In Svendborg (app. 14; q. 4, 9, 10) they have started an electrical bike experiment. The 
purpose should be to get more people to take the bike to work rather than the car and in that 
way minimize the CO2 emission. In the first part of the experiment the municipality leased a 
small amount of bicycles and the citizens could then lent the bikes for free for a week. 
Charlotte (app. 14; q. 9, 20, 29) sent out questionnaires to get evaluations on how the 
experiment had been received.  
The next step was to have the citizens to be responsible for lending out the bikes. Again a 
questionnaire was sent out and afterwards evaluated to see if the experiment should continue. 
In Middelfart municipality they have done it another way. Several of their projects have 
started in buildings that belong to the municipality (app. 13; q. 20). The project has then been 
evaluated internally in the municipality to see if there has occurred any errors or lacks. If this 
was the case these has been changed in the next similar project.  
In Copenhagen they have a different approach to do projects. Søren explains “we seek in our 
project model to make products that are so thoroughly tested that following adjustments 
aren’t necessary” (app. 15; q. 8).  
 
Søren’s (app. 15) team tries to do the testing and evaluation during the developing process, 
where Vibeke’s (app. 13) and Charlotte’s (app. 14) teams finish small parts at a time and 
then evaluate on these. Common for all the municipalities are though that they do 
evaluations at some point during the process.  
 
Nudging 
Both Charlotte (app. 14) and Søren (app. 15) mention nudging as a development method. 
They have specifically used nudging strategies to change the citizen’s behaviour and through 
that satisfied societal goals. The project ‘Ren Kærlighed til KBH’ described in section 
2.3.1.5, was a nudging project conducted by students from Roskilde University, Rostra 
Kommunikation and Copenhagen municipality. The municipality’s use of nudging will be 
==PAGE:60==
 
54 
outlines further in section 4.3.4. Søren outlines ”nudging is an intentional part of our design 
strategy…it is a realisation that we can’t get anything from lifted index fingers and we have 
a lot of people passing through the urban space and therefore we have to get these solutions 
that are based on nudging…as much as we can.”(app. 15; q. 21). This day-to-day running is 
specific for the municipalities and can point to why the municipality have started using 
nudging as a developing strategy.   
 
4.1.1.2$Interaction$with$the$end?user$to$fulfil$end?user$needs$
User involvement 
The municipalities do involve the user both through nudging, but also in several other ways. 
If a bigger project is started there will for instance be a public consultation to get input from 
the citizens (app. 16; q. 20). The Danish government enacts this approach by law. Vibeke 
underpins this by saying that it is important to involve other parties. She explains, “This 
leads to better solutions, get the involved to feel some kind of ownership to the project and 
the project will most likely obtain success and create greater satisfaction among the 
citizens…”(app. 13; q. 7). This points to that the municipality employees are aware of how 
user involvement can create value to both the developing processes and the acceptance of the 
finished product.  
The above shows that there are interaction between the municipality and the end-user, both 
by old fashion ways as public consultations and by newer initiatives like nudging. The 
municipality use some models in the developing processes and some models to include the 
end-user, but hasn’t one particular that most of the municipality use. The challenges from the 
day-to-day running can explain why they are using end-user involvement to a certain degree.  
 
4.1.2 THE ENGINEERING COMPANY 
4.1.2.1$Current$work$process,$models$or$methods$
Work models 
The engineers use significantly more models than the municipality. Probably because their 
specific job is to develop products, where the municipality’s job is to get the municipality 
surroundings to work at a daily basis. Some of the companies use process work models like 
==PAGE:61==
 
55 
SCRUM, stage-gate models or milestones - all comparable to the model by Ulrich and 
Eppinger (2008) that has been described in section 2.2.1.  
 
Several of the engineering companies bring in some kind of strategy model that they focus 
on and believe in. This could be ‘value engineering’ (app. 8) that focus on how to cut down 
on unnecessary parts and surge for value in the project, or ‘front-end’ (app. 10) engineering 
that focus on identifying the opportunities as early in the process as possible. Not one of the 
interviewed engineers uses the same model, even though many of the models build on the 
same principles. None of the models discovered in the interviews are focussing on the 
relationship between societal perspectives and the interaction between the end-user and the 
product. There are though some models that are used by the engineers that are taking the 
societal perspective into account. These are used because the engineers are forced to use 
these models by law. 
 
Models by law 
For some companies there are set up laws that tells what models to use. When designing 
medico products as Technolution (app. 10) and Medicologic (app. 12) does or designing 
buildings like COWI (app. 8), there are severe demands that have to be meet. For instance 
are these companies by law forced to do a risk analysis to ensure that the consequence of a 
failure and the likelihood for this happening are considered. At Medicologic the risk analysis 
is both made on a technical level and a user level (app. 12; q. 18). Thereby they both 
consider the company’s interests and the end-users interests according to safety and health. 
There are though still no models that consider the societal perspective in the interaction 
between end-user and product. 
 
Requirement specifications 
A requirement specification is one element that all of the engineering companies agree on to 
make at an early stage in the project.  
At Medicologic they do the requirement specification in collaboration with the customer 
company. They combine regulatory demands with demands from the customer company to 
make a good requirement specification (app. 12; q. 6).   
Christian (app. 7; q. 11) points to that because the requirement specification often is 
established, it can be an obstacle according to the research in the project. 
==PAGE:62==
 
56 
At Lolle&Nielsen they have another way of doing the requirement specification. As well as 
the other companies they do it together with a customer company, but in the beginning it is 
very roughly written and it is not until later that it is being established (app. 11; q. 13).  
This shows that some of the requirement specifications are made together with a customer 
company, the engineers make some alone and some are not finished until later in the project. 
In this making of the product the engineers would be able to include other initiatives if they 
were aware of what to do and how to include for instance a set of societal perspective 
guidelines.  
Troels from Medicologic mentions that they already use guideline for all kind of things – 
analysis of tolerances, packaging, working drawing, and so on (app. 12; q. 24). Given that 
they already use guidelines, this might be a good way to introduce a new way that includes 
societal goals in the developing process.  
 
Prototypes and testing 
Prototypes and test of prototypes are also used by the most of the interviewed engineers. 
Some make the testing together with the end-users, while others do it themselves. It varies 
from project to project whether the testing makes sense to do in the office using the 
engineers as test persons (app. 12; q. 14) or if the end-users are more evident to include. At 
Eupry they focus on getting some pre-prototypes done as early in the process as possible and 
then test them on the end-users (app. 7; q. 17). By doing this, they find out what the 
important parts or issues in the product are and then they can focus on that. Eupry is thereby 
including the end-user very early in the process and thereby doing a lot of end-user 
involvement. Christian mentions that this strategy also is used as a kind of field study (app. 
7; q. 17).  
Steffen from Lolle&Nielsen outlines that “It is the user experience that is the most important 
when developing products.” (app. 11; q. 20). This points to that the engineers as well as the 
municipality include the end-user to a great extend.  
 
4.1.2.2$ Interaction$ with$ the$ end?user$ to$ fulfil$ end?user$ needs$ /$ inclusion$ of$
societal$goals$
From section 4.1.2.1 above it is distinct to see that the strong connection and interaction 
between the company and the end-user are present in reality as well as in theory. Though it 
doesn’t seam to include a societal perspective when the end-user actions are considered. All 
==PAGE:63==
 
57 
the companies include end-user involvement in some degree and for some of them this part 
is a major part of the development process. But as Frank from COWI puts it; “Every project 
is a compromise involving some parts that has to accommodate one another. When they do 
so it isn’t though always that the intended end-users needs are taken into perspective.” (app. 
8; q. 7).   
This shows a difference between the real life process and the one described in theory (sec. 2), 
because the theory doesn’t have to deal with real world challenges.  
Often there is a third part (customer company), which is the one with the money, who gets to 
decide what to do. This third part could be an entrepreneur, an owner or another company 
that has hired the engineering company to do a job (app. 8; q. 3, 13). This part might not 
have included the end-user or society in their process and thereby it will influence the 
requirement specification and the project description. This could be an obstacle for a set of 
guidelines to work, because the engineers wouldn’t have enough to say to change the 
requirement specification.  
 
4.1.3 Main points 
• The interviews show that models are used in real world development as well as in 
theory. But because they have to fit requirement specifications, time schedules, 
and resources, they are followed to one extend and then skipped. None of the 
models are including societal goals in the interaction between end-user and 
product. 
• The interaction between the end-user and the company are highly developed in 
real world as well as in theory. But as the use of models it depends on resources to 
which extend they interact. The interaction doesn’t say anything about how to get 
the interaction between the end-user and the product to accommodate societal 
goals. 
• The municipality do day-to-day running and this might be the reason for including 
nudging as a development tool as well as end-user involvement in general. 
• The best time to include a model covering the gap between the three actors would 
be in the beginning, because it still will be easy to make changes. All of the 
interviewees use requirement specifications and therefor this could be a good 
place to introduce a new model.   
==PAGE:64==
 
58 
• The customer company could be a possible obstacle to include a new set of 
guidelines, if they have decided on a requirement specification without involving 
the engineering company.  
 
4.2 INVOLVEMENT OF OTHER ACTORS AND 
PERSPECTIVES 
The last part of the interview guide is focusing on how the engineering company and 
municipality include societal goals. This part enlightens how much the engineering company 
and the municipality currently include three different aspects of the societal perspective; 
environmental issues, end-user safety and health, and economy during the user phase. These 
three aspects are described in this section.  
 
4.2.1 THE MUNICIPALITY 
4.2.1.1$Forced$societal$approach$
As a developing unit the municipality are as well as the company forced to follow the 
legislation. Vibeke explains that when the municipality buys equipment for the cities there 
are certain legislations that have to be followed (app. 13; q. 5). Besides this there is for most 
projects some kind of design manual that has to be followed as well. Jens mentions that new 
city equipment has to fit the old style and that there are made very specific rules on how the 
new plant should look (app. 16; q. 6, 16). This involves the colouring, the shaping and the 
material. 
The municipality are also forced to consider legislations about work environment. For 
instance is a municipality worker that empties the city trashcans not allowed to carrying 
more than 15 kilos (app. 16; q. 18). 
 
4.2.1.2$Voluntarily$societal$approach$
The municipalities do several projects that include societal perspectives. As mentioned, 
Svendborg has done a bicycle project to get people to take the bike to work instead of the car 
and thereby emit less CO2 to the air (app. 14; q. 4). Several of the municipalities have a 
division that are specialised in doing environmental friendly initiatives (app. 14; q. 22). In 
==PAGE:65==
 
59 
Copenhagen they try to include ‘cradle to cradle’ in their projects to be environmentally 
friendly (app. 15; q. 13). Vibeke mentions that the day-to-day running is considered during 
the development, to ensure that the work environment and the use are optimal (app. 13; q. 
15, 4). Søren mentions furthermore that they use nudging as a calculated strategy to get the 
products to succeed when the end-users use the products (app. 15; q. 21). 
These examples points to that the municipality consider societal perspectives more than the 
engineering company. This will be clarified further in section 4.3. 
 
4.2.2 THE ENGINEERING COMPANY 
4.2.2.1$Forced$societal$approach$
The Danish government set up some rules that have to be followed when developing 
products. This section will outline how the companies currently are forced to consider rules 
and other regulations from society.  
When looking through the engineer interview material three rules keeps repeating; CE-
marking, risk analysis and usability testing (app. 19). 
All of the engineers that are interviewed, without any exception, mention CE-marking. Jon 
explains that because their medico products by law have to be CE-marked there are certain 
demands that have to be followed in the development process (app. 10; q. 8). For instance 
are they forced to make a usability test and there are strict demands on how to conduct a 
usability test. There are demands on how many people that have to participate in the test, 
demands on documentation, process and so on (app.10; q. 8).  
 
A risk analysis is also a part of the CE-marking. Documentation is required here as well as 
for the usability test. Steffen explains that the risk analysis enlightens what can go wrong, 
how big the risk for something going wrong is and what the consequences would be if it 
went wrong (app. 11; q. 18). He mentions that they besides the risk analysis make use of ‘the 
machine directive’ (maskindirektivet) that sets up clear instructions on what to obtain to get a 
product CE-marked (app. 11; q. 17).  
 
According to health and safety for the user Christian explains: “well, once again, we cover 
ourselves under the CE-marking and that is actually it.” (app. 7; q. 24). Troels is the only 
one that mentions a flaw in the CE-marking. He explains that there is no CE-marking that 
==PAGE:66==
 
60 
mentions how the end-user should be able to use the product easily – it is only meant as a set 
of safety instructions, to prevent the user from getting hurt, not instructions on how to obtain 
a more optimal use (app. 12; q. 16). He gives following example: “What happens if the user 
put in the plug in upside-down – well then it will explode – ok then this will be analysed – ok 
well then you will consider this safety or this risk – well we have designed the plug so that 
the user can’t put it in upside down – as en example. So it isn’t anything about if they can 
manage to plug it in, they just can’t be able to fuck it up.” (app. 12; q. 19). According to 
Jesper this is because it is harder to measure and set up instructions that is dealing with 
something as fluffy as feelings and degree of usability. “There is actually enough money in 
the municipalities, but they all are used for energy projects – the municipalities want to 
invest, but they want to be able to see the time for payback. They want to put in new windows 
or heat pumps…but our unit as it is today is just doing behavioural change.” (app. 9; q. 33). 
This shows that the engineering companies consider the societal goals in the development 
process, but not according to how the end-user interacts with the product. They all use the 
CE-marking as a guideline whether it is CE-marking for usability tests, risk analysis or user 
safety. This shows that the engineering companies all stick closely to what the law demands 
of them, but doesn’t do more to improve on the interaction between end-user and product. 
 
4.2.2.2$Voluntarily$societal$approach$
In the literature review (sec. 2) only one model dealt with the interaction between company 
and society. In reality there isn’t much more interaction. Society of cause sets up laws, 
standards, and regulations that the company has to follow, but some companies also take 
societal perspectives into account voluntarily.   
Many big companies, such as COWI (app. 8), have CSR (Corporate Social Responsibility) 
politics that sets up goals for how the company wants to be social responsible. It could for 
instance 
be 
that 
the 
company 
wound 
not 
make 
use 
of 
child 
labour.  
None of the interviewees from the smaller companies have made a CSR strategy. As 
Christian from Eupry states: “we haven’t looked that much at environmental issues. For a 
relatively new company it is primarily about profit – it is about a good product.”(app. 7; q. 
21). Frank argues that ”someone have to pay environmentally, but we won’t go out and pay 
ourselves for a better Denmark – we can’t do business that way.”(app. 8; q. 24). 
This shows one major issue on why they do not necessarily include societal goals 
voluntarily. The economy is a great player in the game of developing new products. This and 
==PAGE:67==
 
61 
more limitations to developing with societal goals in mind will be outlined in section 4.2.3. 
 
4.2.3 LIMITATIONS TO INCLUDE SOCIETAL GOALS  
The interviews show that the limitations for including societal goals consist of three different 
main aspects: customer company, requirement specification, and economy. They are 
elaborated in the following sections. 
 
4.2.3.1$Customer$Company$and$requirement$specifications 
The customer company plays an important role in the developing process for two reasons. 
First they often are the once to set up the requirement specification and thereby only leave a 
little decision-making to the developing engineers if any at all. “We already know what the 
products should do from the start according to interactions and so on. In the developing 
process it is more a question of putting the right building blocks together.” Christian 
explains (app. 7; q. 13).  
Also Troels (app. 12) and Jon (app. 10) explains that the customer company has the last word 
and often do the requirement specification without involving the engineering company and 
thereby there isn’t much left for the engineers to decide according to environment, economy 
or end-user friendly issues. As Troels puts it “the hearing aid companies just want to be a 
tiny bit better than the competitors, so that the audiologists choose their product.” (app. 12; 
q. 29). Steffen connect to this issue by explaining that the economy in the user phase is 
decided by the property of other similar products (app. 11; q. 19). There are in other words 
no spaces for the engineering companies to involve societal goals, because the customer 
company makes the decisions in advance.  
The customer company is often also responsible for the economics behind the projects. This 
brings the next issue to the table. The economic perspective is just as important for what 
could be a limitation to a new set of guidelines.  
$
4.2.3.1$Economy$
Economy is probably the biggest limitation that comes into play. Every interviewed engineer 
mentions economy in one or more ways. As written earlier Christian outlines that the most 
important for a relatively new company is the profit (app. 7; q. 21). He adds that the 
==PAGE:68==
 
62 
developing process are depending on the customer company and that it is this customer 
company that has the last say, because they are the ones to pay (app. 7). Frank also mentions 
that because the customer company pays the bills, it is the customer company who will make 
the decisions (app. 8; 15). 
  
The economy that is controlled by the customer company influences among others the user 
involvement phase. Troels and Jon both mention that it would be more optimal to do more 
user involvement. ”We ought to go out and find minimum 3, 4, 5, 10 users and ask them how 
the product work today and do all the things we are trained to do – nobody is though 
interested in paying 1000 ddk per hour for us to do so – unfortunately.” (app. 12; q. 12) and 
”…because we as consultants are expensive to hire to do field studies for 3 weeks…”(app. 
10; q. 11). These two statements show a problem about that no one is interested of paying to 
get a proper field study done.  
 
Troels believes that the reason for the customer company to cut down on end-user 
involvement and societal goals is that if they can’t find an economic gain by doing for 
instance environmentally friendly products, then they just won’t do it (app. 12; q. 27). He 
furthermore explains that “if you can’t say that you want to brand differently or our CSR 
strategy would be better this way or if there isn’t some kind of economic incentive or other 
benefit – you just don’t do it because you feel like, because it will take resources to make 
some kind of life cycle analysis.” (app. 12; q. 28). This means that there would have to be 
some kind of encouragement for the customer company to include societal goals. This could 
for instance be a branding value, or an economic gain. 
 
4.2.4 MAIN POINTS 
• The company currently take societal goals into account. They do it to ways: They 
are forced by law to take societal goals into account. This is by doing CE-
markings, usability tests and risk analysis according to the kind of product. They 
also take societal goals into account voluntarily by making CSR strategies for the 
company. It is though mostly big companies that do CSR strategies. 
• The company stick closely to the laws, standards and regulations, but these don’t 
say anything about how the end-user should interact with the product in a better 
way. 
==PAGE:69==
 
63 
• The municipality also follow legislation, but furthermore they have to follow a 
design manual for the specific city. The municipality consider work environment 
laws very careful as well. This might be because they have to handle the 
development phase as well as the maintenance and day-to-day running phase. 
• There are certain limitations to why the company doesn’t include the societal goals 
as it is: 
o The customer companies often are the ones to make the requirement 
specification and thereby the engineering company haven’t any co-
determination. The customer company also controls the money flow and 
what to pay for, which sets a limitation on for instance end-user 
involvement. If the customer company doesn’t have an incentive, as 
branding or economic gain, for including societal goals they won’t do it. 
This influences on the engineering company. 
o The economy as a hole also is a limitation that will prevent the engineering 
company from considering societal goals. The engineering company won’t 
pay the bill if they have to do extraordinary work that won’t benefit 
directly. Time and resources play an important role in the economic 
perspective. 
 
4.3 COMPARISON OF COMPANY AND MUNICIPALITY 
This section is about under which terms the municipality project process is conducted and 
what makes it different from the engineering project process.  
There are four main categories: economy, maintenance and day-to-day running, politics and 
nudging. These categories enlighten four aspects where the terms for the municipality project 
process differ from the company project process. This will show some differences between 
the two actors and thereby give some explanation on why the societal perspectives are more 
included in the municipality process than the engineering company process. 
 
4.3.1 Economy 
Charlotte (app. 14) explains how the project economy works in the municipality. First of all 
they have to make a plan each year to tell how they want to spend the money that they get 
from the Danish state and from taxes in the municipality. The politicians then have to 
==PAGE:70==
 
64 
approve it before they get any money. The Danish government can also demand that a 
certain project is done and they can either give money explicitly to that project or say that the 
money has to come from the already given pool. The municipality don’t have to generate 
money; they ‘just’ have to break even. Because of the fact that they get money and that they 
don’t have to make profit on ‘the business’, they have the luxury to do something 
extraordinary. Søren puts it this way ”we have a unique oppotunity to be pioneers and 
therefor we sometimes decide to use for instance cradle to cradle.” (app. 15; q. 13) and see 
this as a big advantage for the municipalities. Vibeke approve this by explaining that the 
municipality can focus on for instance environmental friendly projects because they don’t 
have to think about profit and often are given money to specific projects like that (app. 13).  
 
These aspects are primarily positive, but there are some aspects that are more negative as 
well. Jens explains that because it is expensive to develop new products, they have to choose 
between already exiting products and might not be able to get something that suits exactly 
(app. 16; q. 3). He also mentions that a lot of the money goes to the ‘soft areas’ such as 
elderly, children and other social areas (app. 16; q. 4). This means that there is cut down on 
the more material projects. Søren mentions that they have to measure if it makes economic 
sense to do the project (app. 15; q. 5). Besides the economy in the development process the 
municipality also have to consider how the equipment is operated in the user phase. ”I am 
also responsible for that the products are able to maintain afterwards – which means that it 
is possible to clean without great additional cost for the municipality, because the 
municipality haven’t enough money to maintain something that is golden.” (app. 16; q. 15). 
A product can’t be too expensive to produce, but certainly neither to maintain. ” Preferably 
we should have some good operational economic solutions.” (app. 13; q. 16).  
These examples show that the municipality has a very different approach to economy, than 
the engineering company, because the municipality get money to do projects, whereas the 
engineering company earn money if they do a good job. 
 
4.3.2 Maintenance and day-to-day running 
Maintenance and day-to-day running is mentioned by all the interviewees from the 
municipalities and is something they consider very carefully. Søren explains that because a 
municipality is a public workplace they have another way of dealing with tasks. Not only do 
they have to develop or order new products, they also have to daily maintenance and running 
==PAGE:71==
 
65 
of the equipment during the user phase (app. 15; 12). They look at the needs of the city and 
then decide what is important to get done. Jens states that an important decision to consider 
when developing or ordering new equipment is if it is resistant of vandalism during the user 
phase (app. 16; q. 7). Jens mentions as an example a whole list of requirements that they take 
into account when ordering new trash cans; easy to maintain and run (to clean and so on), 
easy and cheep to procure replacement parts, easy to operate (the workers are only allowed 
to lift 15 kilos), rein tight, specific colour and material (app. 16; q. 18). He mentions that all 
of these requirements are made on the basis of experience, which is something that the 
municipality possesses because of their daily work with maintenance and running of the 
equipment (app.16; q. 19). Søren mentions “we are dragging around 25 years of technique 
in the city” (app. 15; q. 16) and it means that they have a massive knowledge about what 
works and what doesn’t. The maintenance and day-to-day running is also a forced task that 
has been required by politicians (app. 15; q. 12). The politician’s role will be elaborated 
further in the next section 4.3.3.  
This paragraph shows that the municipality has a great knowledge and many experiences 
about maintenance and day-to-day running that they can use in the development of new 
products. 
 
4.3.3 Politics 
Politics play a powerful role in the municipality’s daily work as well as economy and 
maintenance. The politicians are the ones to decide what the municipality are focussing on 
when doing new projects. They are selected by the citizens and have key issues that they 
focus on when placing specific projects for the municipality to do. A key issue could be an 
environmental friendly profile (app. 13; q. 25) or better conditions for the elderly (app. 16; q. 
4). Charlotte mentions that they for instance have been told to do a waste sorting system in 
the municipalities own buildings (app. 14; q. 14).  
Jens describes a typical key issue according to waste that they have to include in their daily 
work this way; ”we have to get rid of it the cheapest way according to manpower – we have 
to get rid of it in the most environmental way – according to both CO2 – driving – which 
means by car or what we are using, but it also counts when we are getting rid of the waste.” 
(app. 16; q. 21). All these considerations are something they have to do because of what the 
politicians decide. As mentioned in the economy section the municipality has to make a plan 
on how they are going to use the money they are given. It is though not certain that they get 
==PAGE:72==
 
66 
the amount of money that they have asked for. It is the politicians that decide how many 
money a project should have (app. 14; q. 17). This creates an uncertainty in correlation to the 
projects and if it is possible to bring it into action (app. 14).  
 
Regarding the execution of projects the municipality employees have to document what they 
are doing and how the projects are working. ”…It is also the reason why I do questionnaires, 
because it is a demand that I can document the results.” (app. 14; q. 19). Jens also mentions 
that they have to document what they have done to the politicians, but also to interest groups 
that demand to know how the project is done. ”We have to get it done the right way and be 
sure that no one has fiddled to anything” (app. 16; q. 13). The municipality are a public 
institution and therefor they have to put everything into the open and let their work be 
transparent. Engineering companies can hide their work and even have secrets like for 
instance the recipe for Coca Cola. 
If the municipalities are doing bigger projects there has to be a public consultation to ensure 
that the citizens can object if there is anything they are dissatisfied with (app. 16; q. 20). In 
regard to what company to use, a project has to be put out to tender (app. 16) and then the 
municipality has to choose the cheapest offer. In that way the municipality is also limited in 
their work because of the political decisions and the forced documentation.  
The engineering company is all about profit and they can more or less decide what projects 
they want to do (app. 7: q. 21). 
 
4.3.4 Nudging 
The municipalities use nudging as a tool to redesign a product that hasn’t been working 
properly. Søren gives an example of how they have chosen materials for new trashcans in the 
city (app. 15). They have chosen to get the trashcans produced in plastic because the citizens 
then are more carful to put out their cigarettes, because they don’t want to start a fire (app. 
15; q. 20). In theory it wouldn’t matter what material the trashcans were made of, but in 
practice it does. Søren adds to the example ”it is a calculated part of our design strategy to 
include nudging.” (app. 15; q. 21) and ”it is getting used actively in our design process, for 
instance when we design new trashcans.” (app. 15; q. 23).   
The reason for including nudging in the design work is because they have to find design 
solutions that work, when the design is in the user phase (app. 15). ”Nudging is an 
intentional part of our design strategy…it is a realisation that we can’t get anything from 
==PAGE:73==
 
67 
lifted index fingers and we have a lot of people passing through the urban space and 
therefore we have to get these solutions that are based on nudging…as much as we 
can.”(app. 15; q. 21). This shows that Copenhagen municipality to a great extend also 
includes the user phase when developing products to the urban space.  
It is though only Søren (app. 15) and Charlotte (app. 14) that have mentioned nudging as a 
tool they use in the development of new products. Similar for all the municipalities is that 
they to some degree include thoughts about the user phase in the development phase. Both 
thoughts about the end-users and the societal interests like environment.  
 
4.3.5 Main points 
• The following points are where the municipality differs from the engineering 
company. 
• The municipality get money from the state and from taxes and don’t have to make 
profit of their business. 
• The municipality has to budget with both maintenance phase, day-to-day running 
and development phase when doing projects. Thereby the money may be scarce. 
• The politicians within each municipality has two main influences on the execution 
of projects:  
o Politicians have key issues and sets therefor a specific focus on which 
projects that are going to be done. 
o They handle the economy and decide through that which projects that are 
given money and which projects that are not. 
• When the municipality do project they have to document the results to the 
politicians.  
• Two things happens that involve other actors when the municipalities are doing 
projects: 
o When doing bigger projects there have to be a public consultation. 
o If they hire a company to do part of the project, the project has to be put 
out to tender. 
• The municipality use nudging, as well as other methods, to prevent errors 
occurring in the user phase. 
 
==PAGE:74==
 
68 
The interviews have shown that the gap exists in reality as well as in theory. The 
municipality interviews have given an understanding of what is different from engineering 
companies and thereby where to influence the engineering company. This is developed 
further in the discussion (sec. 5). 
 
 
==PAGE:75==
 
69 
5. DISCUSSION 
 
The discussion section will focus on how the information found through the project has laid 
the foundation for a new set of guidelines. The guidelines have been chosen as a method 
because it already is a well-known tool in the engineering development process (app. 4.1.2). 
The first part (sec. 5.1) explains why the reasoning for choice of focus has been chosen and 
why the type of solution has been chosen. Further more it is showed were the guidelines will 
fit in the current process and why they are eligible. The first part also shows how the 
knowledge from the municipality interviews can bee used to develop the guidelines and 
thereby change the development process in a more societal beneficial direction. 
The second part (sec. 5.2) sets the scope for the guidelines to show how they are involved. It 
furthermore explains what the different actors see as good design and where in the process 
the guidelines should be included.  
The third part (sec. 5.3) shows the guidelines and explains why each of them has been 
developed and is eligible. 
In the end of the discussion (sec. 5.5) implications and limitations according to the guidelines 
are discussed.   
 
5.1 REASONING FOR CHOICE OF FOCUS 
5.1.1 THE GAP 
The literature review explored a range of different models. From the figure showing the 
placement, according to interaction, of the different models described in the literature review 
(sec. 2) a gap appears and shows that the interaction between all three actors are limited (the 
part in the middle). The aim of the project is to find a way to include the societal perspective 
in the engineering development process and thereby ensure that this is considered in the 
interaction between end-user and product.  
Given that the literature review as well as the interviews showed that there is little 
information and no models representing all three actors this substantiated the aim to develop 
a model that is able to consider the interests of all thee actors.   
A product that meets all three actors needs would furthermore be the best design for all. The 
feedback interviews backed up the aim as well. Kira (Copenhagen municipality) initiated the 
feedback by explaining ‘the tendency today is very social oriented…so there is a unique 
==PAGE:76==
 
70 
opportunity that the end-user and society overlaps much more than they have ever done 
before’ (app. 21; q. 1) and Søren (Copenhagen municipality) underlines that later in the 
feedback interview ‘if you don’t start doing this, then you haven’t understood the tendency 
that going on in society’ (app. 21; q. 26).  
The gap and the space showing interaction between the company and the user clarified a 
potential to include the societal perspective in the already very explored relationship between 
the company and the user. This is explained further in section 5.1.3. The gap is now clarified, 
but what is the exact problem that occurs when doing social innovation. 
 
5.1.2 SOCIAL INNOVATION 
Tromp et al’s (2011) model is the only model represented in the gap. They put a focus on the 
conflict between collective and individual concerns and this aspect became the second focus 
of the project. The article by Tromp et al (sec. 2.4.2) and the interviews showed several 
examples of issues between social and end-user interests.  
An example from the interviews is from Eupry (app. 20; q. 3, 25) who among others have 
developed a thermometer that is measuring the temperature in refrigerators containing 
vaccines. The thermometer sends measurements to a database during use and thereby 
documents the temperature levels during day and night. The doctors aren’t aware of where to 
place the thermometer in the refrigerator and because they aren’t the ones to pay for the 
vaccines they do not always care about the position of the thermometer. This may cause 
good vaccines to be rejected because the measurements show that the vaccines have been 
exposed to frost because of wrong placement of the thermometer.  
Society wants the vaccines to be kept at the right temperature to be sure that they work 
properly on patients. They furthermore aren’t interested in producing more vaccines, which 
would cost money and environmental impacts, because of inexpedient keeping. This shows 
incongruence between the doctors and society and thereby the individual and societal 
interests. This issue is exactly what Tromp et al (2011) visualises in their model - this 
conflict that occur between collective and individual concerns in the interaction between 
end-use and product. 
   
Tromp et al’s (2011) model shows what the problem is and where it occurs. It is important to 
be aware of the exact problem when having to solve it. Tromp et al’s model visualises the 
==PAGE:77==
 
71 
problem, but it doesn’t give an actually guide to the developers on how to unite the two 
concerns.  
The focus for this project became thereby to close up on a set of guidelines to give the 
developers a possibility to easily consider uniting social and individual concerns in the user 
phase when developing new products.   
 
5.1.3 GUIDELINES 
The literature review showed that there were a lot of models that the engineers can use to 
include the users interests (sec. 2.3). Contrary to this the interviews showed that only a few 
actual models were used to include the end-users (sec. 4.1.2). The main part of the models 
was used because of law regulations (like risk analysis, usability test and CE-markings) (sec. 
4.2.2) or to make the turn around time more efficient (milestone models or stage-gate 
models) (sec. 4.1.2). There is a noticeable different on the amount of models from the 
literature review and the amount of models in the interviews. This difference points to that 
engineers are not likely to use time-consuming and difficult-understanding models in their 
daily work and the method for including societal perspectives would have to be easy to 
understand and easy to use. Troels from Medicologic explained that they already make use 
of different types of guidelines (app. 12; q. 24).  
Guidelines are used for risk analysis, usability testing and CE-markings, in other words; the 
engineers are familiar with guidelines as a tool in the development process.  
The literature review shows a representative selection of design guidelines (sec. 2.2.3). The 
only one of these design guidelines that is purely directed at product development is the one 
by Donald Norman (2013), but as described in the literature review, the design principles 
include many of the same aspects.   
Norman focuses on the breakdowns that happens when a product don’t work intentionally 
(app. 2.2.3.1). He doesn’t include considerations about social and end-user concerns, but he 
looks at how products fail to communicate intended use. As visualised in figure 5 Normans 
guidelines are placed in between the user and the company. They are few, simple and 
understandable and this is a goal for the guidelines presented in this project as well. The 
guidelines will thereby function as solution to the problem outlined in section 5.1.2 above. 
The next step of the discussion is to find out where in the development process a set of 
guidelines would fit. 
 
==PAGE:78==
 
72 
5.1.4 THE DEVELOPMENT PROCESS 
The development process looks different if you look at it as theory or as a real world process. 
The literature review explored several models explaining development processes (sec. 2); 
e.g. the Generic Product Development Process model by Ulrick and Eppinger (2008) or the 8 
steps model by Fogg (2009). All of these models are made step by step like a recipe to 
follow. 
The interviews also reveal how processes are done. At Eupry they use milestones and test to 
develop new products (app. 7; q. 16). At Lolle&Nielsen they used stage-gate models and do 
testing in-house as well as out of the office (app. 11; q. 6, 15).  
The municipality also develops products, but has another take on the development process as 
well. They not only do development of new products, but also have to re-design products 
that aren’t working intentionally (app. 15; q. 12); for instance the new trashcans for 
Copenhagen city. The real life processes are much more fluid than the theoretical once and 
have to be adoptable to the specific project. 
The information from the literature review and the interviews provided an overview of the 
current product process, from the development phase to the redesign of the product. Looking 
at a problem such as the waste that is thrown beside the trashcan instead of in the trashcan 
shows that there is need for including a new aspect in the development phase. A new aspect 
that is considering how to unite societal and end-user concerns.   
 
A visualisation of this issue that occur when societal concerns aren’t included in the  
development process is shown in figure 7.  
 
Figure 7 - The product process from developing to redesign

==PAGE:79==

The model shows how the process needs to do iterations to get a full functioning product in 
the user phase that will fulfil all actors’ needs. The a-arrow represents how the end-user 
interests are included in the current developing process. The b-arrow shows how the process 
has to iterate when a redesign is needed because of unintended use or misuse in the user 
phase. This will cost time, money, resources, that might be possible to avoid using if the c-
arrow is included. The c-arrow is stippled and shows that there is an opportunity to avoid 
iterations if the engineer also considers the potential social problems that might occur in the 
user phase.  
The model shows why it would be giving to include guidelines - about how the product 
might be misunderstood or misused by the user - in the development process and thereby 
avoid the iterations. It might not be possible to avoid iterations entirely, because it is hard to 
foresee all kind of potential problems, but no matter what it would have potential to cut 
down the amount of iterations and thereby save resources for society and indirectly the end-
user and the company. This model shows the eligibility of the guidelines.  
Some of the municipalities already include the c-arrow in their development processes 
because they always have to make sure that the old equipment is maintained as well as they 
are developing new products (app. 15; q. 12) and thereby have a good understanding of what 
is likely to go wrong in the user phase. ”We drag around maybe 25 years of technical 
solutions in the city.” (app. 15; q. 16). This aspect is discussed in the next paragraph. 
 
5.1.5 THE DAY-TO-DAY RUNNING 
During the interviews one conspicuous difference between the engineering product process 
and the municipality product process became clear. The engineers are involved in the 
product process until they deliver it to the customer company or the end-user. The 
municipality on the other hand is involved in the day-to-day running and the maintenance 
phase as well as the product development process, as illustrated in figure 8.    
 
==PAGE:80==
 
74 
 
Figure 8 - The product process shown for the company and the municipality 
 
This model brings together the interview responses (sec. 4) and shows the difference 
between the municipality work scope and the engineering company work scope. The model 
shows furthermore that the municipality has to consider the day-to-day running phase more 
than the engineering company because this is a part that is as important in the work scope of 
the municipality as the development phase. Some engineers might disagree that they don’t 
include the maintenance or day-to-day running phase in their development. This might be 
true to a certain degree, but given that they aren’t responsible for the day-to-day running they 
haven’t got as much experience as the municipality and thereby they haven’t got the same 
foundation for including this aspect. The municipality has an enormous knowledge about 
how the problems occur in the user phase, but also what it will cost if the product isn’t 
working intentionally.  
 
5.1.6 MONEY FLOW 
Another aspect to the day-to-day running phase and the iterations is the money flow. Who is 
going to pay if the product isn’t working intentionally in the user phase? The municipality 
considers for instance vandalism and durability carefully when developing products (app. 16; 
q. 7), among others because they are the ones paying if the product is damaged in the user 
phase. It is also possible that society pays in other aspects as well if the product don’t work 
intentionally; e.g. if the trash isn’t thrown into the trashcan the municipality will have to 
clean up using our tax money to do it. Also if a product is easily damaged and thrown away, 
it will probably end up in the incineration plant and through that emits CO2 to the air. This 
means that a consideration about the interaction between the user and the product in the

illustrated in figure 9.

The figure illustrates how the money flow is in the product process. The stippled box shows 
that if there occur a problem during the interaction between end-user and product, society 
will have to pay. Either in the direct way by e.g. cleaning the streets or in the indirect way, 
where a product is thrown away and incinerated and thereby increasing the CO2 level.  
 
5.1.7 MAIN POINTS 
• A gap occurs both in the theory and in the interviews between the end-user, the 
company and society. Tromp et al (2011) represents a model that furthermore 
specifies the problem to be between individual and collective concerns. 
• The way of solving the problem that has been found is to develop a set of 
guidelines that will help the engineering company to include a societal perspective 
when looking at the interaction between end-user and product. This will help the 
engineer unite the end-user and the societal interests.
